LITERARY NOTES.
Melb. Akgus quotes Lecky as tracing t
word “ socialism ” to a journalist, Reybauc
‘•who gave the party its baptism in t
Revue des Deux Mohdrs.” If Lecky sa
this, Lecky blunders badly. The Revue and
Deux Monties was founded in ’66. There
extant an essay, “ Les Socialistes Modernes
by Jules Breynat, published Paris, ’49.
*sl Colins published three vols. of a wor
called “ Socialisme Rationelle.’’ More, t
Owenites called themselves Socialists in En
land in the twenties ; and C. D. Mauri
wrote a treatise on Christian Socialism
1850. Probably the name “ socialism ”is a
least as old as the centuiy.
• » • •
Two biographies of Parkes are in course o
feverish preparation—one authoritative, th
other quasi-authoritative. And there are
couple of compilations in hand, independen
in origin and purpose. It looks as if, fro
a biographical point of view, Parkes wou
meet Dailey’s fate. The old man, who alwa
anticipated that his life would be written
had a habit of impressing professional scribes
with the idea that he intensely desired h
ghost to return to earth in the word-raimen
of their looms. Hence local heirs to h
literary treasure may possibly be suppl
mented by Gilbert Parker, Christie Murray
and a score of others at the opposite end o
the earth—all of whom could probably alleg
that they were severally made the exclusiv
depositaries of Parkes’s will and testament.

From a novel by an Australian writer, now
in the press :
I am tired of hearing God interpreted by me
who cannot understand His meanest work.
God is a sublime epic paraphrased into th
baldest prose by the pietists.
I would rather suffer from the miscalculation o
an irrational enthusiasm, which achieves litt
though daring much, than gain the negativ
triumph of “no loss” through the miserab
accuracy of the logical prudence which saves a
because it risks uothing.
The manifestation of wisdom is the adjustmen
of a soul or a life to the conditions of its existence
Therefore, we are not called upon by the A 1
Wisdom to lead the heavenly life here.

W. writes :
You are right about the absence of literar
creation by 'Varsity m-n, but does the faul
really lie with the L niversity ? Of course vo
don t expect it to create genius ; but you speak O;
stimulation. W hat stimulation can genius hav
except itself ? What stimulation oould Lawson
(e.g.) have got from any imaginable system o:
University education ? Barring his sins agains
academic correctness, which are frequent enough
I don t know what imperfections any University
would have rid him of. It is only earnestness anc
emotion, or what the academics call inspiration,
that can turn the echo-verse into original sound
and how conld a University training help a man
m either of them ? If the Australian O'Hara,
only write echoes, it is O'Hara’s fault, not th
University’s ; the University couldn’t teach him
to write anything else. If there had been more
in him at the start, there would be more at the
finish. With all its failings, a University course
couldn't crush spantaneity out of a man.
bat makes University men seem such an idea-
less Crowd is the fact that they contain just a&
many woodenheads as the whole of mankind, ba
while men in general aren’t trained to study literary
modeu, \ arsity men are; and the consequent
is that, while only one in a hundred ordinary men
has been taught to reveal his stupidity in lan
guage, with the “educated” class the proportion is
more like one in three or four A University is oi
little to literature, except in that it serves to
keep mediocrity alive, and thereby makes excel-
lence blaze more brightly; but to accuse it of
actual and positive failure, because its graduates
aren’t np to the high water-mark in poetry, is like
growling at the oook because her whinstane soup
hasp t got enough body in it. Give her bones
with some real meat on them, and she’ll dish you
up something better,
• • •
Did “W. never read the parable of the
Sower, whose seed was choked by thorns, or
died among the stones, or fell on fruitful soil
and yielded a hundredfold? Did “ W.”
never see a bicycle-race, with the hump-
backed pedall ers straining after their pace-
makers ? Did “* W. never get lost in the
bush, and suddenly, hungry, thirsty, and
footsore, when heart and flesh were failing,
catch the gleam of a light in the distance ?
A L niversity should be genius’s fertile soil,
surrounding it with all the encouraging influ-
ences of learning and culture. A University-
should be genius’s pace-maker, pointing out
the line of effort, mettling it, stimulating it
by historic examples, drawing it with con-
tagious enthusiasm and hurrying it on to a
definite goal. Genius is often faint and weary:
a University should be the light in its win-
dow. bright with the promise of the ideal,
infusing hope, and confidence, and courage to
struggle anew.
•
What could a University have done for
Lawson Just this. Humanity, slowly
climbing the mountains of Error to the heights
of Truth and Knowledge, has, like Olive
Schreiner's hunter, at infinite cost of blood
and tears, hewn out a stair. A Lniversitv
could have shown Lawson the stair. In the
desert of books a man may wander long
before he finds the path. A Lniversitv
could have shown Lawson the path. In the
sea of facts you may fish long before you find
a pearl. A University could have’ shown
Lawson where the pearls lie. But it is need-
less to multiply metaphors. From readers’
own knowledge must come their conclusions.
The Australian L niversity may be, as “ W.”
suggests, a cook asked to make soup without
stock; or it may be a cook who throws a
scuttleful of wet coals on a budding flame,
6hd extinguishes that flame for ever. Judge !
* » • *
Scribxers Magazine (Gordon and Gotch)
is as a rule rather too American and rather
too refined for the Australian taste. It needs
to be a little more catholic, and a little less
bloodless. But the May issue contains some
really interesting reminiscences of Stevenson
by his step-daughter, Mrs. Strong. Of
Stevenson s visit to Sydney in '93 it is
narrated :
Both Louis and Aolele [Mrs. Stevenson] like to
read trash, that is, if it is bad enough to be funny.
My mother was tired and sent us out to buy some
novels for her. As we went along the street we
saw Louis’s picture in many of the shop-windows,
and people turned and looked after us in a way,
Louis said, that made him feel very self-conscious.
M e went into a big shop and had picked out an
armful of books. A young clerk came up to Louis
with great respect and recognition in his eye.
‘ What have you been getting, Mr. Stevenson ? 1
he asked. ‘We have all the best authors—Mere-
dith, Barrie, Anstey ’ —and then his countenance
changed ; he cast a most reproachful, disappointed
look at Louis as he read the titles of the chosen
works—" The Sin of a Countess,” “ Miriam, the
Avenger,” “The Lady Detective.’’ He retired
and took no further interest in us. We felt it
keenly.
• • * •
Again :
I asked Louis how you could tell good literature
when you saw it.
“It is capable of explanation, I think,” he
said; “ when you see words used to the best pur-
pose—no waste, going tight around a subject.
Also they must be true. My stories are not the
truth, but I try to make my characters act as
they would act in life. 2so detail is too small to
study for truth. Lloyd and I spent five days
weighing money and making calculations for the
treasure found in “The Wrecker.”
I asked him why Charles Reade was not a stylist,
though his writing answered to the description.
1 ' kon are right,” Louis said, “he is a good
writer, and I take off my hat to him with respect.
And yet it was in continuity that be failed. In
the "Ebb Tide,” that is now under way, we
started on a high key, and oh, haven’t we re-
gretted it! If I wanted to say *he kicked his
leg and winked his eye,’ it would be perfectly flat
if I wrote it so. I must pile the colors on to bring
it up to the key. \etl am wrong to liken litera-
ture to painting. It is more like music—which is
time; painting is space. In music you wind in
and out, but always keep in the key : that is, you
carry the hearer to the end without letting him
drop by the way. It winds around and keeps on.
tso must words wind around. Organised and
packed in a mass as it were, tight with words.
Xot too short—phrases rather —no word to spare.
“ There are two kinds of style, the plastic, such
as I have just described; the other, the simple
placing of words together for harmony. The
words should come off the tongue like honev. I
began so as a young man; I had a pretty talent
that way, I must confess.”
I asked him if it were good for the young writer
to wade in emotions.
Good God, no!” he said : “first make his
words go sweet, and if he can t spend an after-
noon turning a single phrase he’d better give up
the profession of literature.”
• * » *
Pedagogue : “ The English text set for
this year s matriculation exam, at Melb.
T niversity did not need the title-page to
show it was edited by a Melb. graduate. All
the notes are a re-hash of those which Prof.
Morris is wont to serve out at lectures : and
the provincial schoolmaster, who has been
accustomed to astound his pupils with reams
of literary lore (which, by the time it reached
him. must have been about fifth-hand) is now
deprived of his whole stock-in-trade, and is
reduced to gibing fiercely at the annotator s
unnecessary length.”

From Ai.wa Mater (the Melb. University
magazine) :
We would like to see a more general interest
taken in the social life of the University bv the
Dons. The products of a mere “ stew shop,” such
as the L niversity threatens to become, are not the
materials whereby nations are built. In this new
country, with so much pioneering work, social and
political, to be done, we must have robust men
and women, a result which a crowd of book-worms
can never yield.

-. uL , : 4 of the ‘Australian School
Series is an unadnnrable compound of
savoury themes and original grammar. One
lesson is entitled ‘Pure Water’ and has
several delicate allusions to cess-pits (p. 39).
Another choice essay on ‘ Vermin : lavs
special stress on the species found in dirtv
people's heads, with directions for getting
rid of it ‘ (p. 204) and dwells lovingly on
bugs (p. 203). A description of a porcupine
scratching ticks out of its ear is included in
another edifying narrative (p. 34). In the
lesson on ‘ Alcohol ’ (which has a marked tee-
total tendency) you read (p. 161) «Beer in
the form of ale and porter, cider and perry !
contain alcohol, but generally in small pn>
portions, and are used as beverages by great
numbers of people.’ Almost as elegantly
worded is the paragraph in ‘ Presence of
Mind (p. 73) :— * A great king . . . had
commenced the narration of a story to his
assembled courtiers, when he suddenly
noticed among them a person who would
have been pained by continuing the tale.’
Cheerful— and classic 1 Who was to continue
the tale 1 ”
I held the man for a decent, intelligent
laborer, whom it was a privilege to know,
but I had never suspected him of poetica
sensibility. Yet he was literally drunk
with the Night and the poem (we
hadn’t tapped the whisky). He raved, he
implored, and his frantic gestures nearlv
upset the boat. Presently he grew quieter,
came down to water again, and we fishec
about for a long time till we found the sculls.
Then my friend explained. He had rear
the poem in a newspaper : it was the one
poem he knew : it enshrined his own life-
romance. To him it seemed “ the greatest
poem ever written.”
* » » •
When David Christie Murray a short time
afterwards deliberately expressed the opinion
that ” My Queen of Dreams ’ contained “ the
most essentially poetic verses ” he had found
in the Australian Anthology, the impression
left upon me by this incident was deepened.
Here were a rugged, hard-handed toiler and
a clever writer, well-versed in literature, who
reached from different points of view an
identical conclusion. Fuller acquaintance
with Mr. Holdsworth’s work showed that he
has written poems which deserve to be long
remembered by Australia.
* * • *
Mr. Holdsworth s single book of poems
was published by W. Maddock in ’BS, and is
now out of print. The dedication runs : “As
a young Australian, I dedicate this small
volume of poems to my fellow-countrymen
of Australasia, less, perhaps, for its own sake
than as an earnest of future effort.” Ai : for
the dreams of youth. The volume was warmlv
praised on its appearance, but of late years
it has dropped out of notice. Yet it'con-
tains a good deal of decent verse, and three
or four poems whose appeal could hardly be
stronger. Chief of these is “My Queen of
Dreams,” a lyric brim-full of exquisite emo-
tion. Another is “ Quis Separabit?” of
which only the final verse, first written, is
included in the book. The whole poem was
subsequently printed in Sydney Tkuth dur-
ing W. H. Traill’s editorship.
This is fine rhetoric, but hardly so good as
poetry, and one feels a touch of artifice anc
affectation. It is an oratorical poem : jusi
speak the second stanza with a round mouth
and a little sympathy ! A chief quality o:
Mr. Holdsworth’s verse, indeed, is its
sonority : one would say he composed aloud.
He is distinctly an ear-poet, and to get his
full flavor you must recite him. Recurring
to a recent comment on this page, compare
Holdsworth with Victor Daley, a poet whose
verse apj>eaLs strongly to the eye. Here is
a beautiful picture from one of Daley’s finest
poems :—
Spellbound by a sweet fantasy
At evenglow I stand
Beside a strange sardonyx sea
That rings a sunset land.
The rich lights fade ontone by one,
Like to a peony,
And drowned in wine, the round red sun
Sinks down in that strange sea.
With red clouds has he cb&pleted
His brows, like him who tied
Of old a rose-wreath round his head,
And drank to Death, and died.
Try to recite this, and in the very first verse
one is caught in a cacophony of sibilants.
Nevertheless, it is highly musical—with the
music of colour. Mr. Holdsworth’s is the
music of sound—his rhythms and vocables
commingle with a ringing chime like a
sweetly-tempered peal of bells. In “Loth
to Depart, ” another fine emotional lyric, he
rises almost to the height of the" pieces
quoted.
• « • ,
Mr. Holdsworth’s longer narrative poems
—“Station Hunting on the Warrego ” and
“At the Galley of the Popran ” are the
chief — have occasional fine phrases and
passages, but are not so good in their way.
The former is written in blank verse—now
so rarely tried in Australia —and this passage
illustrates its quality :—
(Ah, God I what fierce extremes encompass life!
Note yon plump Sybarite, whose noons are feasts
Whose midnights dainty banquets! Hedged with
gold,
He sucks abundance from Earth’s shores and
seas :
He drains the Wine of Life from jewelled cups,
And fattens weil for grave-worms !
. . Different fares
1 on child of pain that treads dry, furnaced tracts !
Above, stretch skies of fire : around him, plains.
Bare, moistureless : beneath him, earth — his
grave!
For him no rich looms ply with curious skill;
No menials crook and cringe ; no tempting cates
Nor rare wine* glisten : at Fate’s Sibyl-hand
He plucks Desire, Mistrust, Hope,' Fear and
Death !
Ah, God ! what fierce extremes encompass Life!)
» » • $
Mr. Holdsworth is most successful when
he is most sincere. Too often his work is
tainted with self-consciousness, and in verses
like “ Love’s Lamentation ” —
Day drooped his plumes of gold : hoarse fiends of
air
Sprang up with clamorous mirth,
Loosed the red whirlwinds from their thunder-
lair,
And ravaged sea and earth :—
Fear’s palsying films my dim, strained s;ght be-
dewed,
(Higher the bleak brine surged !)
For in the storm s blind march, Love’s sail I
viewed,
M ind-driven, sea-scourged !—
one seems to catch sight of a Prt'ciexix posing.
M hen a wave of deep feeling submerges his
Other-Self. Mr. Holdsworth's work is inevi-
tably fine. The pity is that this happens
rarely. There is a sense of artistic youth
throughout the book—in the comparative
deficiency of intellectual quality, in the
exaltation of sentiment, in the too-luxuriant
ornament, in the continual twang of allitera-
tion. Lines like—
As a traif driven rild by hell’s rAirlwinds, I am
wrecked on the drunkard’s dire shoals—
jar on taste and ear alike. But youth, with
all its limitations, gives passionate glow, and
Mr. Holdsworth has a sense of words so rich
that he is able at his best to concentrate
the white-heat of passion in a white-heat of
language. And if he does not always satisfy
the head, he is sometimes able to touch the
heart with a key which unlocks all its magic
chambers—a key which the Muse has never
yet entrusted to ungracious pastors of Pre-
cision.
» • • •
The following recent and notable lines
by Mr. Holdsworth (here printed for the
first time) hold a full measure of his charac-
teristic charm :—
Printed that a few years ago Single-taxer
Henry George was left £4OOO, but his
friend’s widow opposed the bequest. She
died in the poorhouse recently, which put an
end to the suit. George has now received
£4o—the balance of £3060 being swallowed
up in legal expenses. This should s
stimulus for another “Progress and PoveL\ '
lawyers progress and clients poverty.
Philip J. Holdsworth.
Writes “Helicon”: Its periodical flood
had reached an Australian country town-
ship. The river had risen and risen
till the backwater covered all the low-
lying areas, sent adrift the dwellers in
hundreds of cottages, and surged well up
into the principal street. The rain had
stopped days before, but the water showed
no sign of receding. Night came, and a
moon mellow, magnificent. I took a boat at
the door of the local “ theatre,” picked up a
man near where the ridge-pole of a church
just showed above the water, pulled through
the door of an adjacent hotel, and. kept
the boat s nose against the bar-counter
while the landlord took down a flask of
whisk}’ from a top shelf, then turned out
again down a side-street, past the
dotted house-roofs, and away over the
calm expanse. There was no wind,
no current, and the evening-star hardly
trembled in the water. The trees on
the river-bank just showed their topmost
boughs, and on the hills the township lights
twinkled. For half-an-hour we rowed on
silently, and “drew the quiet Night into our
blood. Then my companion suddenly
rose in the bows, letting his oars drift, and
proceeded to recite
MY QUEEN OF DREAMS.
In the warm flushed heart of the rose-red West,
When the great sun quivered and died to-day,
You pulsed, O star, by yon pine-clad crest—
And throbbed till the bright eve ashened grey—
Then I saw you swim
By the shadowy rim
Where the grey gum dips to the western plain,
And you rayed delight
As you winged your flight
To the mystic spheres where your kinsmen reign !
O star, did you see her ? My queen of dreams
Was it you that glimmered the night we strayed
A month ago by these scented streams ?
Half-checked by the litter the musk-buds made?
Did you sleep or wake ?
Ah, for love’s sweet sake,
(Though the world should fail, and the soft stars
wane !)
I shall dream delight
Till our souls take flight
To the mystic spheres where your kinsmen reign.
“QUIS SEPAR ABIT?"
All my life's short years had been stern and
steril—
I stood like one whom the blasts blow back.
As with Shipmen whirled through tbe straits of
Peril,
So fierce foes menaced my everv track.
Bat I steeled my soul to a strong’ endeavor,
I bared my brow as the sharp strokes fell,
And I said to my heart— “ Hope on ! Hope ever-
Have Courage—Courage, and all is welL" *
Them bright as the blood in my heart’s rich chalice,
0 Blossom, Blossom I—you came from far •
And life rang joy, till the World's loud malioe
Shrilled to the edge of our utmost star.
And I cried : ** On me let the rough storms
hurtle,
The great clouds gather and shroud ruv sun—
But you shall be Queen where the rose and mvrtle
Laugh with the year till the year is done. ’
So myOream fell dead; and tbe fluctuant passion—
The stress and strain of the past re-grew
The world laughed on in its heedless fashion.
But earth whirled worthless, because of you.
ak ? Tears which my grief discovered,
1 laid dead Love with a passionate kiss.
And over those soundless depths has hovered
The sweet, sad wraith of my banished bliss.
Heart clings to heart—let the strange years sever
The fates of two who had met to Dart:
Love’s strength survive*, and the harsh world never
bball crush the passion of heart for heart •
F ° r v l ,^ ow my thoQ £ b i* droop and dwindle,
fehall leave me Love till I fade and die,
And when hereafter our souls rc-kindle,’
Who shall be fonder —you or I!
WHY SHOULD MT SWEET LOVE
SORROW?
Why should my sweet Love sorrow? If it be
A grief to love—that grief falls most on me :
baall cold earth throb, and greet :ae strong Sun’s

And I not thrill for thee, my Star, my Sweet?

Which brings me wedded to my Heart's Delight!


Medical.
COUGHS, COLDS ON THE CHEST, BRON-
CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE. —Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing t hat for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much worse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of

made bright. Don’t waste valuable time.
If you suffer from any, or many, of the following
symptoms, don’t delay a moment. Write and receive
prompt reply:—Depression of spirits, bashfulness, in-
ability to look frankly into the eyes of another, head-
ache, hair coming out, dim sight, noises in head and
ears, weak memory, forgetting dates, persons’ names,
places, etc., loss of voice, taste, or smell, sunken
eyes, pimples on face, paleness of face and lips, look
old for years, stunted in growth, palpitation of heart,
pain in or under breastbone, shortness of breath, in-
digestion, with oppression after food, constipation
or irregular bowels, flatulency, gravel, weakness or
pain across small of back, loss of muscular power,
gloomy, remorseful, fearful of something going to
happen, disturbed sleep, moaning, talking, grinding
teeth, tearful or want to laugh.
I have known instances in which
most of these symptoms were present
in one patient. As a rule a great
many are present T u no case are
many absent.
Dr. WALLACE,

BEFORE YOU
LJBAJP!
When you go out shopping, consider, think
of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer —for you to lead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*

You Are No Poet—You’re Not
Dead.
(Apropos of some Recent Criticisms.)
You write a bold and dashing song
With all the fire of chivalry,
With all a good man’s hate of wrong
And scorn for all the crimes you see.
Your song may throb with righteous wrath,
Rise with a noble feeling grand,
And fall with woes that haunt the path
The poor tread in this Southern Land.
But this is merely common verse—
“ Malodorous rot,” the critics said,
The stuff that demagogues rehearse ;
You are no poet—you’re not dead.
You catch the weird and solemn tones
The grim Australian deserts breathe,
You learn the song the forest moans,
The strain the troubled waters seethe.
You hear a whisper from the spheres,
The sad, eternal stars o’erhead,
That look in laughter and in tears
Till this strange race called man is dead.
You weave their golden-glinting rays
Into a web of gorgeous sheen,
Where Southern constellations blaze,
Where glorious wonder-worlds are seen.
I can’t admire the song you sing,
The critic cries, and shakes his head ;
The man who died could do the thing—
You are no poet —you’re not dead.
You hear the song the drovers raise,
You ride with them through storm and shine,
Your verse glows with the roaring blaze,
The camp-fire crackles in each line.
Your song shakes with the rushing feet,
Wherever tread the strong and bold
Upon the long and dusty beat
That leads them to the land of gold.
The sheep-dog’s bark, the clash of shears,
The scent of wool from Castlereagh ;
The merry din which greets your ears
You send your readers far away.
You tell a story well, ’tis true,
In lilting verse, the critics said;
But what we have to keep in view
Is this—that you are not yet dead.
When bushrangers are dead and gone
A century of years or so,
And drovers, too, have followed on
With shearers to the camp below;
When all the bush is “civilised ”
And towns are busy far inland,
Your works will be again revised
When people do not understand
What were the themes you wrote about,
Or what a drover’s toil and care,
Or if the shearer was the lout
Who cut the first Australians’ hair.
What was the “ Bush,” at any rate ?
Then full of years and mystery—
Your names, no doubt, will all be great:
At present you’re alive, you see.
The grave’s your place, the critics sing,
Ye poets of the heart and head,
The men who died wrote everything ;
You cannot write —you are not dead.
Melb.
R. STEWART.
Mill : “Is not the circumstance of the
poor respectable 73-year-old woman drowning her-
self, because there is no room in Melb. Benevolent
Asylum, an argument for Woman Suffrage—at all
events, for the placing of women on the board
there ? Look at the crowd of hearty-looking men
in that same Benevolent Asylum, and nothing like
73 years old.”
A.M.P. Teece suggests that Sydney central
railway-station should be somewhere about the
site of Govt. House. “ There are plenty of better
sites for Govt. House.” Certainly— Woolloomoo-
loo, for example; or in the Centennial Park,
where “the nicest people ” disport themselves on
the bike in “the nicest way so far as the horrid
democracy will let them. For at the inaugural
meeting of the Hampden Cycling Club bold, bad
members of the Surry ’llls “ push ” didn’t scruple
to drive up in dirty little sulkies drawn by scraggy
little ponies, and ogle the nicest people as they
pedalled around ; while ladies from “ the Rocks ”
came to stand by and make uncomplimentary re-
marks about calves that wanted stuffing. If
Govt. House is shifted to Centennial Park, Lord
Hampden, as the club’s patron, will doubtless
arrange for the nicest people to exercise in the
nicest way in his new grounds, with all nasty
people shut out by a nice ten-foot fence. Mr.
Teece ’s suggestion is really the nicest of all, and
is bound to be warmly supported by the nicest
| people.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form, of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing icith Aus-
tralian subjects, a>id not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 woids, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sendiitg MS. to distinctly understand that

SATURDAY, JUNE 13, 1896.
An Unreported Case.
O-YE of the casual straws which show the
direction of the current of justice transpired
—so far as a straw can be said to transpire—
very recently in the Sydney law-courts. It
was in itself a small, mean, commonplace
case concerning an obscure informant, and
some bad beer, and an allegedly trickv
hrm of brewers ; but it is interesting as
showing how things don’t happen, and how
many possibilities there are of blocking the
course of law and of press publicity when
the right people feel that way inclined. The
persons accused were Tooth and Co., the
well-known Sydney brewers, a limited lia-
bility concern of great wealth and respect-
ability, and with an influential body of
shareholders. The charge laid, or attempted
to be laid, was one of swindling the public
revenue by fraudulently obtaining draw-
backs—in other words, of getting, by certain
false pretences, refunds of excise-duty on
liquor that had gone bad in the brewery
and become unsaleable, said refunds
being more than the company was fairly
entitled to. The charge was set forth
in great detail and sworn to by the
informant, and on the surface of things it
certainly looked like a matter which called
for inquiry. Whether or not the plaintiffs’
allegations against this most reputable firm
were ill-founded The Bulletin doesn’t pre-
tend to know ; all that is known is that
the case was never tried. The chamber
magistrate refused to issue a summons against
the influential beerfirm. The Supreme Court
decided that it had no power to interfere.
The Justice Department refused to take
any steps in the matter ; and the daily
papers, though their reporters were in atten-
dance, quietly omitted all mention of the
matter from first to last, though the hearing
of the application to the Full Court for the
issue of a criminal summons occupied a whole
morning, and was the means of postponing
the sensational Meagher case for some days'!
All of which was so utterly unlike the usual
processes of N.S.W. law and journalism, as
to arouse a suspicion that an influential
brewery - concern stands on a somewhat
different level from the ordinary or garden
variety of defendant.
The proceedings began on 2nd December
last by an attempt to lay an information
before Magistrate Paytex, of Sydney. In
the case of a cheap and nasty defendant a
summons almost automatically follows the
laying of an information. When an ap-
parently sane plaintiff, with an address at
which he can be found when wanted, and a
respectable legal adviser, and a circumstantial
tale to tell, is prepared to swear that an
offence has been committed, the law almost
invariably assumes that he should be allowed
to prove his charge if he is able to do so,
and that the accused should likewise be
given a public opportunity of establishing
his innocence ; in the present instance, how-
ever, the magistrate refused to issue the
summons until he had consulted with and
been advised by two high Customs officials.
And apparently the said officials decided
against the issue of a summons, for it was
ultimately refused. The said officials were
hemselves in some measure defendants in
he case: the information charged Tooth
and Co. directly with defrauding the revenue ;
therefore it indirectly charged the Customs
department with having allowed the revenue
to be defrauded. This is at least one reason
why any ordinary Customs official might be
glad to hear nothing more about the case,
and whj 7 the decision should not have been
left with the Customs at all. The decision
that there was really no fraud at all, and not
sufficient semblance of one to call for inquiry,
seems to have been arrived at by the Customs
Department sitting as a secret court of
inquiry ; having appeared before itself, it
unanimously acquitted both the directly and
the indirectly accused—the latter consisting
>f itself.
* * * *
Magistrate Paytex is the only functionary
o whom the application could be made in the
irst instance, and when he refused—ap-
larently on the advice, as already
hown, of some more or less interested
parties —to move in the matter, the
proceedings, in the ordinary course of
affairs, came to a deadlock. The next
step was therefore to apply to the Supreme
Court for an order directing the magistrate
to issue the summons. And the Supreme
Court decided, upon the strength of an
English precedent, to refuse to interfere in
the matter. Therefore, it appears that Mr.
Paytex is an autocrat who can ignore a
sworn information and prevent even a pre-
liminary public enquiry into what may, or
may not, be a matter of great public import-
ance. If this decision is good law, then there
is one more law in New South Wales which
badly needs revision. In a case where the
Attorney-General shows no sign of moving ;
and the magistracy refuses—on the advice of
more or less indirectly interested officials,
whose judgment may be unconsciously biassed
by their position—to let a private individual
move ; and the officials in question, having
already helped to block the private prose-
cutor, can’t be expected to move themselves,
it appears that there is absolutely no means
of compelling a public investigation. A
system whereby one very small official can
smother an accusation—whether it be well-
founded or otherwise—before it even begins
to see the light, contains so many possibili-
ties of evil that it needs to be promptly
suppressed.
* * * •
For some reason or other the proceedings
in the Supreme Court were left wholly unre-
ported in the daily papers. Whether this
was done by direction of the Court or by the
press on its own account The Buxletix
doesn’t know for certain, but either way it
shows a remarkable tenderness for the feel-
ings of the big brewery co., such as smaller
concerns, which haven’t a paid-up capital of
£900,000 and £68,000 of reserves, seldom
experience. When the matter cropped up
in the Assembly the results were about
equally satisfactory. The questions asked
and the replies of the Minister were as
follows :
1. Was an application made recently to a city magis-
trate for the issue of a criminal summons against Messrs
Tooth and Co., brewers ? 2. If so, what were the grounds
of the application ? 3. What reason does the mao-istrate
assign for refusing to issue the summons? Mr. Gould
answered 1. Yes ; for a summons for obtaining monev
b>’ false pretences. 2. On the ground that the Slid com-
pany obtained a refund of £53 11s., paid by them for dutv
on beer, on their fraudulent representation that the beer
had become bad, and had been destroyed at the com-
pany s brewery. 3. As the alleged fraud concerned the
Consolidated Revenue, the Collector of Customs was con-
sulted upon the matter, and that officer explained that
full inquiry had been made by his department, with the
result that he had come to the conclusion that no fraud
had been perpetrated, but that, in fact, a sum slightlv in
excess of the duty required bad been paid, and that if
there had been any suspicion of fraud the Customs Depart-
ment would have taken action. In this refusal I fullv
concurred.
* * * *
This is an elaborate evasion of the whole
question. The charge was not that Tooth
and Co. had made a “ fraudulent representa-
tion that the beer had become bad ; ” the
question at issue was whether they hadn’t
obtained a refund of excise-duty on certain
liquors which had gone bad, but some of
which had never paid excise at all. Also,
while the complainant gave his reasons in
full for believing or professing to believe that'
a fraud had been perpetrated the Customs
department apparently gave no reasons for
believing that it had not. Further, the
allegation that Tooth and Co. had paid
rather more than the duty which was legally
due, had no earthly connection with the
matter, and no one had said anything to the
contrary ; the question was whether they did
or did not claim and receive back again more
of the duty originally paid than they
were entitled to. The statement that they
had originally paid the proper amount
was as irrelevant as an assurance that the
principal shareholders had duly liquidated
their dog-tax. The question as to the magis-
trate’s reason for blocking all inquiry was
not answered at all ; it was merely stated
that the Customs department wasn’t moving
in the matter itself, which fact was already
known, whereas the real point at issue was
the action of some person or other in pre-
venting a private individual moving at his
own risk and on his own responsibility The
law, as a rule, allows any person to take
action against any other person, provided the
accuser seems to be clothed and in his right
mind, and his solicitor is in the same de-
sirable state, and he is prepared to allege
that a crime has been committed. Then it
sets the machine in motion, and supplies a
judge to see that the two parties fight the
matter out publicly according to certain
fixed rules ; and its refusal in the present
case to let the machine move at all looks
most remarkable.
* * * *
The amount at issue is certainly a very
small one. The case was also a small one.
The complainant, so far as The Bulletin
knows, is a person of no more importance
than the average, and the reputation of
Tooth & Co. is such that the public would
be greatly astonished to find the charges
verified. The whole matter is only interest-
ing because it arouses a suspicion that
the law has powers not before suspected
to smother inquiry, provided it cares to
use them. In the present instance, a distinct
and serious allegation was made which in-
directly affected certain public officials, and
the magistrate smothered it at the outset,
allegedly on the advice of these same officials.
Then the Supreme Court decided that it was
wholly at the mercy of the said magistrate.
The Customs Department declines to move
publicly in the matter ; to do so would be to
allege that it had been badly taken in, which
would be unpleasant in any case, and besides
the Customs Department seldom moves pub-
licly, but compounds with offenders against
the tariff law by fines that are paid more or
less secretly. The Crown Law department
doesn’t move, apparently on the advice of
the Customs department ; and it also refuses
to know what the charge really is, and gives
reasons that have no earthly connection with
the matter. Altogether the whole case,
whatever the merits of it might be if they
were threshed out, is an interesting evidence
of how the law and the press and the Govern-
ment can sit on the head of a small plaintiff
who is trying to proceed against a very large
defendant, if it feels so inclined, and of how
things sometimes don’t happen in New South
Wales.
The “Lands for Closer Settlement”
Scheme.
Is Xew South Wales, where a little over one
million people are scattered loosely over
198,000,000 acres of country, land available for
settlement has already become scarce. So
much of the coastal district has been jobbed
away in immense areas to speculators, squatters
and syndicates that, despite the general empti-
ness of the province, there is practically no
land now obtainable on which the agriculturist
can make a living—assuming, that is, that he
desires to live as a civilised being and not
degenerate to the level of the aboriginal or the
Polynesian beachcomber. There is abundance
of vacaut soil to be had “out back,” but it is
not available in any reasonable sense. Land
available for agricultural purposes must, in the
first place, be reasonably well-watered, and in
the western regions water is scarce and drought
plentiful. It must be in a district moderately
well supplied with roads, and in the
regions where land is to be had at a not
absolutely-impossible price roads are few
and bad, and the Govt, can’t supply good
ones for want of population to pav for
their maintenance. Also, it must have cheap
railway communication with the centres of
population, which all lie on or near the coast,
else the agriculturist can’t compete with the
importer of foreign produce blessed, as he is,
with comparatively inexpensive sea carriage ;
and to make railway communication cheap °he
land must be situated comparatively near the
sea-board. L nder these circumstances the-
Reid Government has been moved to introduce
a measure called the “ Lands for Closer Settle-
ment Bill,” which authorises the State to re-
purchase large properties suitable for agricul-
tural purposes, and re-sell or lease them on easy
terms to small settlers. The proposed measure
has so far been received with unexpected
enthusiasm in many quarters where such enthu-
siasm was wholly unexpected. It is a very
short document, which professes to aim at the
same purpose as the land-resumption law of
Maoriland, and the way it contrives to avoid
getting straight at that purpose, and the manner
in which it deviates into bye-ways of suspicion
and possible boodle are remarkable.
* * * *
in the first place it does not definitely appear
that the measure gives the State any powers of
compulsory acquisition, therefore it starts with
a most conspicuous failure. If the Ministry
desires to obtain a certain property it is simply
authorised to “ enter into provisional agree-
ments with the owners of the said land as to
purchase thereof or compensation therefor to-
gether with any improvements which may be
situated thereon.” If the agreements ‘ are
ratified by Parliament then, after certain other
formalities, the purchase of the land may pro-
ceed. As the Ministry can’t enter into an
agieement with anybody unless the person in-
volved also enters into an agreement with the
Ministry, it would seem that the matter is
purely optional on both sides, and the seller
need not agree at all unless he gets his own
price. YY hen this difficulty is surmounted, the
Ministry, having entered into the agreement,
is bound to assure Parliament that it is a good
and square bargain ; and as the direct Govern-
ment party will probably support it under all
circumstances, because it is its business to sup-
port the Government under all circumstances
and many members of other parties won’t
oppose because they have no personal knowledge
of the matter, or because they also have land
to sell to the Government, and as the Govern-
ment generally has a majority in any case the
matter will, in most instances, go through’win,
little difficulty. But the first essential is that
the State must offer a price which will induce
the owner to sell. And as the trouble to b
remedied is not the want of good land, nor th
want of owners who are willing to sell’ but th*
want of owners who will sell at anything even
faintly resembling what their property is worth
this arrangement doesn’t appear to advance
matters much. The would-be agriculturist can
get land already if he is prepared to pay the
same owner, or another just like him, a high
enough price to induce him to sell—which he
isn’t, because the price is so high as to be nr»„
tically prohibitive. P ac '
• * * .
The mode of purchase, in fact, is not verv
widely different from that hitherto adopted in
the case of lands required for railway or other
public purposes. The sale not being compul
sory, the matter practically resolves itself into
a scramble between the .State and the land
owner, and the general results of that method
in the past have been that the State paid from
two to twelve tunes the fair market-value of
the property. The only improvement intro-
duced is the power of revision by Parliament
which may amount to much or little or almost
nothing, according to circumstances. But even
this power is more apparent than real The
second clause of the Bill provides that the
Government may, if it feels so inclined, resume
land, apparently in the same good old way which
has led to so much disaster in the past, leaving
the compensation to he settled as it is at present
when resumptions are made for railway or
other public purposes. This certainly ap-
pears to mean that the settlement will
be . arrived at by private agreement, or
arbitration, or by a jury, and these are exactly
the means whereby the State has been systema-
tically robbed on an enormous scale in years
gone by. The bill itself contains no explana-
tion as to why the Ministry demands this ex-
traordinary power of acting outside Parlia-
mentary authority ; it provides, in the first
place, that the Legislature shall at least have
the power of placing its veto on any transaction
that looks more than usually nefarious; and,
iu the second place, that the Legislature shall
have no veto worth mentioning unless the
Ministry feels so inclined. Premier Reid adds
a little further darkness to the matter by the
following explanation which he offered to the
Assembly on the second reading debate :
It was true that the power of resumption had proved
verv- dangerous in the past. No one had a more vivid
recollection of the enormous amount of public money
which had been sacrificed under powers of resumption
than he had. But these dangers practically disappeared
in the proposal before the House, for the reason that the
power of resumption was only intended to be applied with
reference to lands which had been the subject of pro-
visional agreement, as was provided in the earlier part of
the bill. The clause providing for resumption stated that
the Governor may resume any lands referred to in the
“ aforesaid notification.” That notification was published
in the Gazette after all the conditions and safeguards in
the bill had been carried out. First of all, there was the
provisional agreement between the Minister and owner;
secondly, the submission of the agreement to both Houses
of Parliament; and thirdly, the approval of the Houses
having full information. Only after these proceedings
could the notification appear in the Gazette authorising
the Governor to resume. The danger of land resumption
of former days was almost entirely, if not altogether, got
rid of under the bilL
» * * »
From these obscure explanations it appears that
after the price of the land has been agreed upon,
and the Legislature has sanctioned the purchase
at the aforesaid price, the Government may, if
it pleases, complete the purchase on the terms
approved by Parliament. Or it may, for no
visible reason, “ resume ” the land with no re-
ference whatever to the terms fixed by Parlia-
ment, and leave the compensation to be after-
wards decided by private agreement, or arbitra-
tion, or by the award of a jury—which award
may be several times the amount sanctioned by
the Legislature. Therefore the Legislature
may authorise a purchase solely because the
land is offered at <£lo,ooo, and by virtue of
its authorisation the purchase may be completed
at .£50,000. If this is what the bill means
it is about the most amazing proviso
ever inserted in any Act of Parliament.
And if this isn’t what it means then it is diffi-
cult to understand that the clause in question
contains any meaning whatever. The Bill
states, it is true, that the process of resumption
may be resorted to “if necessary,” but it
doesn’t define the necessity, which matter,
apparently, is left to the discretion of the
Ministry. And if the land under consideration
happens to be the property, sub ro*a, of a
member or supporter or relation or business
connection of the Ministry, and Parliament
refuses to take it except at a price much below
the owner’s idea of its value, the power to
decide that the aforesaid necessity has some-
how arisen, and thereby to remove the question
of price wholly’ out of the jurisdiction of Parlia- •
ment, may prove of great importance. More-
over, the Bill declares that “the exercise of the
power of resumption shall not be subject to any
other condition precedent than the aforesaid
notification in the Gazette.” Therefore it is
an open question whether the Bill doesn’t give
power to resume land without the matter being
laid before Parliament at all—in other words,
whether it doesn’t authorise the Government to
run the whole business without consulting any-
body.
* * * *
The amount to be expended on land pur'
chases in any one year is limited to £300,000,
and this sum is authorised to be raised by the
sale of debentures bearing interest at not more
than 4 per cent, with a currency of not more than
40 years. But it is further provided that, if the
seller of the land is agreeable, lie may receive
the debentures in payment, instead of the
money raised by their sale in the open market.
This opens up another brilliant prospect of pos-
sible boodle. At present N.!>. V\. 4 per cent,
debentures are worth <£ll2 in the open market-
If the Ministry is negotiating the purchase of a
large estate for, say, ,£200,000, it may raise the
money by selling .£ISO,OOO worth of 4 per cent,
debentures at- a shade over ,£lll net. Or »
may, if it feels particularly good towards the


eller, pay him with 4 per cent, debentures for
£200,000, the selling value of which would be
£224,000. There is no evidence, of course,
i hat any Ministry would use this power, but,
at any rate, the power exists, and it certainly
should not exist. In the case of payment by
debentures either the 4 per cent, limit should be
cut down to, at most, 3£, or it should be ex-
pressly stipulated that the State securities shall
he taken, not at their nominal amount, but at
their current selling price in the local market.
Otherwise the transaction may be a private
sale, without competition, of valuable securities
at very much below their real value.
* * * *
The whole policy of the Bill, in short, is
vicious and objectionable, and full of possibili-
ties of future evil. The Crown should, in the
first place, be endowed with the power of com-
pulsory purchase if any good is to result. The
valuation should be the land-tax valuation, with
such an amount added—whether it be 10, or
15, or even 20 per cent. —as Parliament shall
consider fair compensation for disturbance.
Under the old resumption system New South
Wales has seen the value of a piece of land
sworn to for probate purposes at, say, £5 an
acre, and the same land sworn to by the same
valuers a few months later for State purposes
at several times the amount, and it has also
paid for it on this inflated basis. Under the
new resumption system it will one month see
values sworn to for land-tax purposes at £1 an
acre, and the next it will be called upon to re-
purchase the same property at twice or three
times that figure, and this new species of fraud,
which is only a variation of the old fraud of
which the country is so weary, requires to be
suppressed with a strong hand. By assuming
the land - tax valuation, with a certain
fixed margin, as the standard, Parliament
will at least take the matter to a great
extent out of the range of boodle
and political influence and secret negotia-
tion. Also, the administration of the Act
should be placed, not in the hands of the Minis-
try, but in those of permanent officers, so that
Parliament cannot be driven to agree to a
nefarious bargain lest it should thereby overturn
the Government, and bring in a new fiscal
system, and lose the benefit of all the unfinished
legislation then on hand. Under the system of
Government by Contradiction Parliament has
often allowed a Ministry to do objection-
able things because the only alternative
was the ejection of the entire Cabinet, and
because the only alternative after that was
a new Cabinet composed of men who had a
tendency to do still worse things, and past ex-
perience in this direction supplies good evidence
that the powers of the Government for doing
works of boodle should not be further enlarged.
And, in the present case, the unexpected en-
thusiasm shown by many of the opponents of
t he Reid Ministry, and the cordial rallying to
its standard of the veteran syndicators and
land-jobbers of the Assembly, is a powerful
reason why the country should think specially
hard over the matter. When the landowners
and the loan and mortgage men and the squatters
and the embarrassed banks unanimously approve
of a scheme for selling land to the Government,
the community naturally wonders whose land
is going to be sold and at what price.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Aggressive J. Bull.
The commercial world awaits with interest the
decision of the Vic. Full Court as to whether
the English reconstruction of the N.Z. L. and
M.A. Co. is binding on Yic. creditors. The
primary Judge, after considerable argument,
had decided that it was not. In the event of
his judgment being upheld, it is the intention
of the co. to appeal to the Privy Council.
There are expressions in the British Bank-
ruptcy Acts which leave no doubt that, when
the British Parliament legislated on certain
bankruptcy proceedings analogous to these, it
legislated for the whole Empire. Such expres-
sions are wanting in the British Companies
Acts, which on their face appear to be purely
local. The contentions of the co. are of more
than legal importance, as may be gathered from
the fact that Todd’s “ Parliamentary Govern-
ment in the Colonies ” is one of the books cited
by its opponents. Pushed to an extreme, in-
deed (not a hard matter, in law), the position
contended for might be made a powerful lever
for loosening the foundation of the qualified
local independence we are supposed to enjoy in
Australia. We are at present subject, without
representation of any kind, to all sorts of English
legislation, framed from the point of view of
English interests solely, on bankruptcy,
naturalisation, merchant shipping, marriage,
extradition, patents, copyright, trademarks,
and a host of other such subjects ; but there
lias always been a kind of unwritten law to the
effect that on any other subjects we were not
affected unless we were expressly named. We
are not named in this Companies’ Arrangement
Act, and, although it may seem just enough
from one point of view, that the same law
should be in force with regard to bankrupt
companies and bankrupt individuals, from
another point of view it is most undesirable
that the whole empire might be held, by any
tinpot court in it, to be affected by any loosely-
drafted provision in any obscure Act, perhaps
even an inaccessible private Act, just because it
was enacted by the Parliament dominant of
that small part of the said empire known as
England. Even as it is, Australians would be
astounded if they knew the number of impor-
tant Acts, affecting them often in their daily
lives, in the making of which they have had no
say whatever, and of which they are utterly
unaware until, under the provisions of an Eng-
lish extradition Act, they are dragged back
under arrest to Adelaide, say, for going to Mel
bourne on a visit while owing a S.A. tailor’s
bill. The theory of the dominance of the British
Parliament is capable of being carried to
such extraordinary lengths as it is, that any
extension of it in the way sought in the present
case may reasonably be viewed with positive
alarm. If the expression “ all creditors, ” in a
local English Companies’ Act, is held to apply by
implication toall creditorsin the British Empire,
and to bind them, what is to prevent “ all
persons shall lift their hats to the squire ” in
some English “ Act for the Encouragement of
Toadstools ” being construed to apply to Wool-
loomooloo or Collingwood ? Anyhow, this case
and others like it serve to show us in Australia
that there is a good deal of humbug about our
boasts of being self-governing concerns, and the
sooner we wake to the danger of the delusion
the better.
The Position of Trustee Co’s.
The experiment of allowing companies to
undertake work hitherto confined by English
law to individuals is now several years old, and,
judging by dividends, has been fairly success-
ful. But both the original South African
experiment and our own have, and will have,
the troubles and petty annoyances incident to
innovations. The law refuses to give these
companies an inch more rope than is allowed
them in the Acts constituting them, and, as a
consequence, new legislation is continually made
necessary to give them complete status as
trustees. A point which was raised without
being decided in the Melbourne Supreme
Court lately illustrates the caution of the
Courts, while at the same time it indicates to
the public the kind of pitfalls likely to be
hidden in the regions of company trusteeship. A
man had appointed his wife and a trustee co.
joint executors of his will. The co. claimed
the usual commission, which was resisted by
the wife on the ground that the co’s Act didn’t
permit it to act as joint executor, and that,
therefore, as companies joint execu-
tors before the Act was passed, they couldn’t now.
The Judge was struck with the point, and, as
hundreds of estates are in the same position,
offered to get the opinion of the Full Court in
the matter ; but the case was settled on another
ground. The public’s interest is obvious,
and, having regard to this particular type of
case and to mauy others which may possibly
turn out to be instances of unauthorised com-
panies meddling in administrations, it would be
interesting to knowhow things really stand. The
whole question of satisfactory guarantees for
the absolute contingent solvency of all institu-
tions such as insurance and trustee companies,
banks and building societies, which live on and
by public confidence supported by Parliament-
ary endorsement, needs to be re-opened and
re-modelled. It shouldn’t be possible that we
should have to witness, in the fullness of
time, the same sort of crashes in connection
with trustee or insurance companies as have
been experienced in the case of banking and
other public trading companies. However rosy
things may be now, cholera and administration
actions are always possible, and are about
equally deadly on their respective feeding-
grounds when they get fairly to work.
The Claim of Brother Mike.
A bulky and expensive lawsuit was heard re-
cently in Melbourne to decide whether or not a
man now deceased had transferred his property,
valued at £l6OO, to his wife by “undue in-
fluence ” on her part. There were no children,
so the deceased’s brother Michael, who
appeared as plaintiff, was entitled to a share
in the estate if the transfer to the old woman
could be upset. This sort of thing lends weight
to The Bulletin’s theory that where there are
no children a widow should have first claim on
the assets of her deceased husband up to, say,
£5OOO, that being an amount which, invested in
State securities, would support an ordinary
widow decently. In most cases when the aged
house-father leaves a little bit of land behind him
at his death, he and his wife have pegged away
together to accumulate the property. And
when the property, as generally happens, is
barely sufficient to maintain the old lady for
the remainder of her days, the law which
enables the deceased’s brother Mike to claim
half of it, and more or less burst-up the
little estate in law-expenses to enforce his claim,
looks rather one-sided. Even if the widow
had nothing to do with the acquirement of the
property, she had at least to put up with the
deceased, and old men w 7 ho leave property be-
hind them are generally disagreeable and rather
hard to put up with, and unpicturesque to boot.
Moreover, brother Mike, in the majority of
instances, had nothing to do with the accumu-
lation of the property either, so where his
claim comes in against the widow is difficult
to understand. The present law is contrary to
public policy, for brother Mike, in most cases,
can support himself, and the widow, in most
cases, can’t; and by depriving her of half the
estate, especially if there is very little estate
altogether, she is often accelerated into the
Benevolent Asylum, where she becomes an
expense to the community. When a citizen
departs this life, leaving no w ill, no children, a
widow, and a few hundred pounds, and after
his funeral expenses are paid the widow gets
half what is left, and all the old man’s remote
relatives divide the remainder, and the expenses
of hunting up ail the relatives are taken out of
the estate before it is divided, there is very
little satisfaction in the business for anybody.
And, in any case, this world is a cold place for
any elderly widow, so The Bulletin votes
against Brother Mike in all his aspects.
If the N.S.W. cement industry, practically
closed up, makes a fresh start, it must be on
the basis of about 10 hours’ work per day and
about 30 per cent, less wages. The stereotyped
Foreign-trader insists on professing that Pro-
tectionists demand Protection as a mere tem-
porary expedient to be abandoned as soon as a
new industry gets fairly afloat, and they want
to know, in a derisive tone, if Protection isn’t a
failure, seeing that after many years of tariff
support the cement industry can no more stand
alone than it could at its inception. Which is
about as logical as wanting to know why a
country should maintain a police-force for pro-
tection against criminals, if after maintaining a
police-force for many years it is no more able to
get on without it than it was at the beginning.
In an industry which is exposed to foreign com-
petition Protection is wanted until the local
men are willing to do as much work for as little
pay as the cheapest of their foreign competitors,
which the Labor-party naturally objects to ; or
until all foreign workmen reach the Australian
wage and hour standard, which seems a long
way off; or until freights rise so high that they
make up for the difference in cost of production,
which, as freights keep going down, seems still
further off; or until the world’s supply falls so
far behind the demand as to raise prices to the
point at which the industry can stand paying
the Australian rate of wages, which event is
only possible in a very few industries. And if
none of these things happen, either Protection
must go on for ever or the unprotected industry
must perish.

A Bulletin correspondent who is in a position
to know what he is writing about: —
There are a good many things in the Vic. rail-system
which require Commissioner Mathieson’s attention. The
ordinary safety-margin in matters of maintenance, the
present officers to the contrary, notwithstanding, has
been shamefully narrowed. It is a fact, notorious in the
western district, that engines running daily from the
Geelong depot go often for weeks without a touch from
the cleaners’ waste. The chance of detecting a flaw in
the machinery, under such circumstances, the public may
estimate for themselves. The state of passenger rolling-
stock is eloquently portrayed in this ; that during last
Easter holidays the roof of a carriage became partially
detached while running and hung only by one corner till
repaired at a country station. The junior clerks of the
department are cruelly sweated. Parliament insisted last
session that men on low salaries should receive incre-
ments. They have received them, but involving as
they 7 have done loss of pay for Sunday work,
the poor devils find, in many cases, that the
“increments” resulted in an actual reduction in jay.
Men are employed discharging coal from trucks at Port
Fairy at the rate of twopence per ton. Eightpenee is con-
sidered a reasonable price for the work, yet the Govt,
goes abroad loudly to suppress sweating ! Political in-
fluence is as rife as ever it was. All kinds of people—
mostly those who can well afford to pay—travel here and
there and everywhere on Parliamentary passes. A concert
was , held in a Gippsland electorate last week —the local
member in chair - two singers were brought from Mel-
bourne for the occasion and both travelled on the divinely
vague and magic cardboard. This, under ordinary 7 circs.,
is unjust, but taken in conjunction with the facts that
employes are virtually robbed to make the railways pay,
it is positively indecent. —Yours, Dargs.
A bold declaration of the comparative insig-
nificance of the poor man, according to law,
was made by Judge Chomley, at Horsham,
Vic., the other day, in a case of seduction. The
girl and her father, a selector, sued a local
squatter, and his Honor awarded ,£75 damages.
Quote :
His Honor said that, were the plaintiff not a man in a
very humble position in life, and practically without
means, the damages would be much greater. The con-
duct of the defendant towards him had been very gross,
and such as warranted a measure of damages far in excess
of the merely technical loss sustained. There was not
one law for the rich and another for the poor, but, in
awarding damages to them, the circumstances of parties
and the value of money to them must be taken into ac-
count.
So, if a man is earning £lOO a year, his] daugh-
ter’s honor is worth £75 ; if he is earning
£lO,OOO, it is worth £7500 ; and yet it must
not be thought for a moment that there is one
law for the rich and another for the poor—
Heavens, no ! The spirit of the law that esti-
mates the pain of a broken heart pro rata with
the income of the owner is identical with the
Tory-principle of the property-vote, and it is a
melancholy thing to find both prevailing in an
alleged democratic community.
A few months ago a N.S.W. country parish-
priest contracted for certain additions to his
church. Before the job was finished a garnishee
order for an old judgment against the contractor
was served on the priest. The contract was
thrown up, and finished by the priest, against
whom the workmen employed immediately got
attachment-orders for wages due by the con-
tractor. The contractor had been paid all the
money he was legally entitled to draw at the
time he surrendered the contract — i.e., 75 per
cent, on the work done. The priest had only
enough money on hand, after completing the
work, to pay half the claims, and he did not
know where to begin: to pay the old debt
on the garnishee-order or to pay the men
who worked daily before his face under a back-
country December sun. He thought the wages
claim came first, and accordingly put in a
defence against the first garnishee-order in the
interests of the workers—especially as there
was actually no money legally payable (though
due) to the contractor when the first garnishee-
order was served. The case came before Judge
Gibson, who did not decide it in court, but
sent up his decision from Sydney after some
time. This was that the first order for an old
debt was binding, as the money was accruing
to the contractor, and all the costs were given
against the priest. Thus a contractor’s old
debt is given precedence against wages on a
current contract, and the laborers lose their
hardly-earned money A crying injustice. A
contractor’s workmen should have first and
absolute lien on the profits of his contract. As
N.S. W. law says otherwise, Parliament should
ticket that law for alterations.
Under N.S.W Education Act, clergymen of
any denomination are allowed an hour per week
in which to impart religious instruction to the
scholars. The practice is, on these occasions,
for the teachers to cull from the whole school
all the children who profess the brand of
religion which the visiting parson has on tap ;
these are marched into a separate class-room
and left, for the time being, in the minister’s
sole charge. Meanwhile, none of the classes
outside has a full complement of scholars, and
the teachers, under the circumstances, naturally
refrain from giving any important lessons. They
simply while away the hour. And, as some of
the large metropolitan schools have an average
of three parsons interrupting the school in this
manner three different times weekly, the whole
school thus literally loses at least three hours
of its teaching-time every week. But often the
loss of time doesn’t end here. There is the
time lost in drafting the- youngsters into the
religious-instruction class, and in getting them
back again into their respective classes ; and
over and above this, the time that the parson
himself wastes by not turning up at the ap-
pointed hour, and when he doesn’t turn up at
all, as often happens. Were the Act altered
so to allow of religious instruction being
given after school-hours only, it would meet a
long-felt want.
Svdney D.T., in recognising editorially “the
impotence of parliamentary government,” sug-
gests the small reform that more Bills should
be originated in the N.S.W. Leg. Council. The
questions involved are much larger. The red-
tape “constitooshun ” of “party government ”
—Government by Contradiction ” —needs to be
asphyxiated. An elective government, each
member of the administration to be balloted for
by “ the House ” ; the abolition of the Attor-
ney-General as a political potentate; the
abolition altogether of the portfolios of the
Minister of Justice and the Postmaster-General,
making the working departments only re-
sponsible to (say) the Chief Secretary; the
abolition probably of one or two other redun-
dant portfolios ; the_ making it possible for
“ the House ” to kick out one Minister without
touching his colleagues ; the appointment of
committees to shape and present Bills to “ the
House,” after the fashion of lowly municipali-
ties ; and a rigorous set of rules to curtail the
eloquence of jawbone members —are among a
few of the needed reforms that at once present
themselves to the mind. The Tory “organs,”
however, only sec that the nominee chamber of
irresponsible soniles should have more and more
power, and demand that the Constitution
should be amended solely on its behalf.
Victorian women don’t seem as plucky as
their English congeners in claiming their pos-
sible rights. On the flimsiest of grounds,
sometimes, English women have sought and
gained election to boards and councils through
some cranny of the law. Occasionally, o
course, they’ve been thwarted. That’s all in
the game. But although Victorian women
have a strong prima facie case on the question
of their eligibility as members of the Assembly,
no one of them has yet raised the requisite
pluck, or cash, or public spirit, or whatever
it is, to storm the hustings. The position
appears to be as follows : The Constitution
Amendment Act provides that a candidate
for election to the Legislative Council
must be a male person with certain quali-
fications, and that an elector must be a
male person with certain other qualifications.
The same Act also provides that an elector for
the Assembly must be a male person, but that
a candidate for membership of the Assembly
must be a subject of the Queen with certain
qualifications, of which sex does not appear to
be one. Male electors for Council and Assembly,
male candidates for Council, but merely sub-
jects of the Queen as candidates for the Assem-
bly. The inference certainly is, especially if
the Acts Interpretation Act is dragged in, that
women are eligible as candidates for the Assem-
bly. Of course, the courts may have a say in
the matter, or the Parliament itself, and that
may mean much. “ Male ” may be rejected as
“surplusage,” the Acts Interpretation Acts
may “ not apply ” or be held to be ultra vires
in this connection, the lex et consuetudo Parlia-
ment may galvanise itself for this occasion only,
the intention of the framers of the Constitution
Act and Statute may be dragged neek-and-crop
from the vasty deep, but for all that the letter
of the Act is in favor of the she-M.L.A., if she
likes to try to avail herself of it.
Victorian Artists’ Exhibition.
The Yio. Artists’ Society is just now exhibiting a
small collection of pictures described as “per-
manent.” This permanency is subject to pur-
chases, removals and exchanges of one picture for
another, but seeing that some of the exhibits are
likely to remain on the premises as long as they
can find hanging-space the show is quite perman-
ent enough. In the meantime, it has been spread
around the Society’s best room, and deserves
respectful notice. Gov. Brassey performed the
opening ceremony, anyway. Also, he did honor
to the occasion by buying one of president
Mather’s pictures for 60 guineas.
Mather’s picture aforesaid is a comparatively
poor sample of his skill, entitled “ Near Cape
Schank ” —unlovely rocks, sea, and sky, such as
many a worse artist might treat more feelingly.
The breakage of water upon the reef is denoted
by a large upstanding blob of white paint, solid
as the head on a pint of ale. Governor B. should
easily be able to get better value from the presi-
dent’s brush at some future time. “ The Dawn of
Night,” by J. Forde Patterson, will satisfy the
veteran’s admirers. Darkness is creeping over a
tall, thin slice of Gippsland mountain-range, and
a dull green sense of reality comes from the canvas.
F. M‘Cubbin contributes another of his large,
misty, yet gaudy Victorian landscapes—“ A Bush
Camp ” —in which the commonplace figures are
well situated and not badly drawn, whilst the
effect of sunshine through the trees is distinctly
clever. Nature and M'Cubbinism struggle for
supremacy in this meritorious work, and the con-
test terminates in a draw.
“ When Daylight Dies ” is a convincing expanse
of sea at sunset time, with three children paddling
in the foreground. The youthful figures belong
to the impressionist school, being featureless and
unfinished. Had Tudor St. G. Tucker felt equal
to endowing the bathers with enthralling faces he
would have done so, no doubt, but he preferred to
leave them in an early stage of art. Another sea-
scape by Tucker is less pleasing. “ Autumn,” by
A. Boyd, is an impression. It might be the com-
mencement of a good, bad, or indifferent picture,
it may be praised by cranks, it can’t be taken
seriously by the public. These remarks apply to
two alleged portraits, or studies of human faces,
signed Helen A. Peters. Millais, or a competent
signboard-writer, might have painted them as far
as they go, for they have stopped short at the point
where preliminary proceedings terminate and artis-
tic effort begins. And so it is with several specimens
of Young Victorian landscape work hanging up for
sale. The trees are smudges, the skies are daubs,
the colors are not more final than the first coat of
drab slapped upon a summer-house which is going
to be green day after to-morrow. David Davies
scores with his “Breezes and Breakers,” as regards
the reflected light on a moving water. But the
sky is flat and painty and the distance that lends
enchantment to the view has not been arrived at.
A portrait of Professor Tucker’s little daughter, by
E. P. Fox, is a pretty work, although not to be
compared, for strength, truth, and carefulness of
execution, with Longstaff’s picture of his wife.
This is a soulful lesson in unaffected art, before
which the rising generation of Victorian painters—
whose work, for the most part, shows more talent;
than modesty—should doff its Alpine hat.
