The Rev. Chas. Clark, who has filled
Centenary Hall all the past week, is still
undoubtedly what he was before—the greatest of
living lecturers. The Rev. Charles is growing
old, but he looks almost as young as he did many
years ago; his wonderful voice rings out as strongly
as ever, and his elocutionary powers have in no
way diminished. In this respect he is an anom-
aly in the clerical profession, for the elocution of
the average parson is awful, and if he went mad
and bit the congregation at random he could
hardlv fail to take a piece out of a better speaker
than himself. The Rev. Clark’s greatest successes
up-to-date have been his lectures on Dickens. He
apparently knows the great .novelist by heart,
and also the inmost soul of his characters.
His sketch of Squeers, and the impassioned
manner in which he made a pint of milk
go round eight boys, mostly orphans, is a piece
of acting which Dot Boucicault might be proud of,
and his other characters are equally effective. The
number of different persons which this lone parson
contrives to be in the course of one evening, with-
out scenery or props, and with no costume save
his own orthodox black “clobber,” is a striking
proof of versatility—especially when it is remem-
bered that the average reverend never manages to
be anybody but himself, and generally does that
very badly. . And when he gets on to description,
his word-painting is wonderfully rich and pictur-
esque ; he either knows all his subjects to perfec-
tion or has the art of seeming to do so, which is
much the same thing after all. The Rev. Clark’s
series covers four more lectures. To-night (Thurs-
day) he holds forth on “Thackeray”; Friday
on “St. Paul’s”; while Saturday and Monday
are devoted more or less to Dickens.
* * * *
“The Wages of Sin,” which is doing a
one-week season at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), is a
production which, on the whole, Reserves better
luck than it has had up to date. There is a lot of
large moral thrill in it; it tends to show that the
wages of sin are paid regularly; and the steady
decadence of Alfred Boothman' until he becomes
an unshaven ghoul hiding in a cellar is a serious
warning to all young men. Boothman at the
beginning is a gaudy clerk with a white hat, and a
blue waistcoat, and brown boots, and a black soul.
When the girl he has betrayed returns with her
hair hanging down her back he persuades her to
lay her sin on the curate, which is Alfred Woods ;
whereupon the curate's best girl marries Booth-
man, and he drinks all her money, and stoushes
her in the eye beside her dying child, who is
pegging-out in something like a bath in an attic.
Then the wife goes thieving to obtain victuals for
the infant and gets into gaol, and Boothman
stoushes her again in a cellar, and her life
is one large blue bruise from beginning to
end. Boothman is decidedly effective as the
ruffian in the cellar, where his bleared eye
looks like a lantern shining on a wreck;
and Woods makes the parson nearly as
respectable as anybody in the orthodox
long-tail coat can be. The stage parson
is never an individual to be taken seriously.
About the best features of the show, how-
ever, are Miss Austen Eliot as a widow
who is named Bloggs (and looks it), and Mrs.
Harry Marshall as daughter Juliana. There is
much wild humor in Widow Bloggs and her
daughter Juliana, and they serve to light up the
gloom of the drama considerably. Miss Edith
Barrow, as an airy creature with two large black
eyes, assists in the same good work. As for the
heroine, she lives her bruised life in the attics and
cellars, and is much stoushed, and run in, and
dragged out again, and burn pied hard on various
floors, and all Miss Wilde’s abilities can’t make
her anything better than the tearful wreck she is.
A weak feature of this heroine is that she is alwavs
telling her dissipated husband that she won’t let
him do something, whereupon he hits her in a
fresh place and does the thing without much diffi-
culty. Most of the male part of the show, barring
Woods and Boothman, badly wants a large quan-
tity of something heavy dropped on it frequentlv
from a great height—sav 1376 feet,

Brough and Boucicault have arrived at the
scrapv-end of their season, and are filling in the
last days of the partnership with a series of
revivals. Oscar Wilde’s * Ideal Husband " occu-
pied three nights. The eerie “Mps. Ebbsmith”
came next on the list, and the cold winds of
despair whistled through the old dry bones of the
sex-problem once more, and the psychological
whatsisname wandered forth to commune with
the soulful and desolate something or other about
that something else which never seems to get anv
better defined. Next week is to be filled in with
brief spells of “The Passport,” “The Idler,”
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray.” “The Magis-
trate ” and “ The Amazons,” and after that
Dion Boucicault passes over the Australian
horizon for an indefinite pieriod. Meanwhile,
the solemn fact must be acknowledged that the
B. and B. farewell season is planning out none too
well Sydney is suffering just now with a violent
Tendency towards melodrama, and, considering'
how badly high-class comedy is supported at pre-
sent, The Bulletin suggests that it would be a
tender and appropriate piece of irony if the Cri-
terion season ended with one night of “ His
Natural Life " —the goriest wrsion. By potting
on Titheradge as Gabbett, and Ward as Troke,
and Mrs. Brough as the thin convict, the firm
might unobtrusively express its opinion of the
average Sydney audience, and insult it in a quiet
way which it would probably not fully drop to till
a week or more after the eo. had departed.
** * *
A testimonial to Dot Boucicault is under
way, and an influential committee has the matter
in hand. No canvass is to be made for subscrip-
tions, but those who have a cash reverence for the
brilliant actor and unrivalled dramatic tutor are
invited to send their bullion to Mr. A. Hoffnung
(of Hoffnung & Co.) treasurer to the fund. Also,
as the time available is short, like Dot himself,
they are invited to send it at once.

The garish magnificence of ‘ ‘ The Derby
Winner ” at Sydney Royal hasn’t faded yet,
and Miss Watson’s historic swear stands up pic-
turesquely in the drama like a lone curse in the
middle of the Old Man Plain. A further inspec-
tion of Bland Holt's piece has made The Bulletin
feel better towards Miss W. than it did last week.
Her solitary curse grows on one, in a manner of
speaking, or at least it did until the management
took it out of the show, and thereby re-
moved a really bright and effective remark.
It was a short, crisp, and effective “damn,”
and when it was interpolated by a penitent
woman weeping on her knees in the centre of a
gaudy drawing-room there was a suddenness about
it which added interest to an otherwise not unusual
observation. The adventuress, taking her all
round, is hard and masculine, and addicted to
warming at the fire portions of herself which her
sex doesn’t generally treat in that fashion, also to
striking attitudes which exhibit the soles of her
shoes to the audience, and she doesn’t look at all
the fascinating creature she is alleged to be. But,
for the sake of that one eloquent swear, a good
deal might be excused—if the management hadn’t
taken it out.

The Rickards show at Sydney Tivoli is
well and strong, and Fitzgeralds’ Circus still pros-

Apropos to the reappearance of Mrs.
Potter and Bellew, at Melbourne Princess’s, The
Bclletix must mention, with regret, that W.
Shakespeare's comedy, “ As You Like It,” is more
palpably ridiculous than of yore. It lost its last
hold upon credulity when the modern virgin took
to cycling. No fellow can imagine himself unable
to recognise his best girl as his best girl in the
event of her adopting “ rational ” dress. If
Orlando becomes suddenly enamored of Rosalind
at a ball he isn't to be persuaded that she is a
strange young man when he meets her next week,
in knickers and gaiters, falling off a bike. Rosa-
lind’s deception of the love sick wrestler is much
too thin for these times, and the same remark
applies to Touchstone’s philosophical jokes,
whieh wouldn’t be tolerated from a nineteenth-
century circus-clown. Even a banished duke
would object to such hollow mockeries of
humor. “ As You Like It ” is a famous
piece of literature, profusely praised and
freely quoted, also irreproachable in sentiment,
but it grows more absurdly unconvincing every
year. As a poetical farce, brightened by fancy
costumes and first-class woodland scenery, the
Bard s masterpiece seems a dreary entertainment
compared with “The Amazons.” This criticism
is both Gothic and up-to-date.
* » * a
The “As You Like It ” show discloses no
difference in Mrs. Potter and Bellew. The lady’s
personality remains always fascinating, and her
style generally theatrical, as before. Perhaps her
bursts of tender naturalism are rather more fre-
quent, and her voice rounder. Mrs. Potter still
persists in talking English with a foreign peculi-
arity, whilst her eye speaks the universal lan-
guage of the soul with liquid eloquence. But
from the face downwards this Rosalind is
altogether eclipsed by the memory of Mias
Essie Jenyns, her assumption of male attire
suggesting rather a North American Indian
or Robinson Crusoe, just according to how she
happens to strike one at first. Kyrle Bellew has
preserved all the previous effectiveness of his
clean-cut oountenance and expressive legs. More-
over, he treats Shakespeare’s wordy lines with
reverent discretion. So do most of the other
people, for that matter. Scot Inglis, as Jaques,
is excellent, in a heavy way ; Geo. Ireland, J. F.
Cathcart, Edwin Kelly, and Cyril Keightley fill
their places ; Elton tries hard to make Touchstone
endurable; and Miss Polly Emery is a riotous,
lively Audrey. Two or three members of the
company range from moderate to feeble, also the
singing is far below average form, and quite un-
worthy of the staging. The arrangement, at time
of writing, was to carry “As You Like It ” over
a second week—sad to say.

“ Silver King,” at the Royal, progresses
favorably with two important changes in the cast,
Scot Inglis and Elton having been called away by
another engagement. King Hedlev, the successor
to Inglis, plays the name-part in a plain, straight-
forward maimer, which is sometimes the best
manner possible and sometimes not. Hedley is
a disappointing actor, inasmuch as he shows all
the symptoms of intending to let himself go pre-
sently, but doesn’t fulfill the promise when the
time comes. Nevertheless his moderation in all
things and thorough knowledge of business make
him respected. Stirling Whyte has succeeded
Elton, as Jaikes, to a considerable extent, although
there is room for him to succeed some more.
Otherwise “ Silver King ” is unchanged, and the
united efforts of the eo. and the scene-painter
deserve the same praise as usual.

The Frantz Family acrobats, who duly
appeared at Melb. Opera House last Thursday,
are individually and collectively superior to any
other congregation of muscular Christians ever seen
in this country, not even excepting the Craggs.
The Craggs were perfect in their work, the
Frantz Family, a little less neat, perform more
feats at a faster pace. Also, three of them
are full-grown women, in full-length skirts and
bodices cut low, and the spectacle of these
smiling amazons turning somersaults, and deal-
ing with one another as though they were
sacks of coal, is gorgeous in the extreme. The
Samson of the Family is a fair-haired matron who
holds her liege lord above her head with one of her
dear little hands, and seems equal to shifting a
steam-roller with one playful nudge of her dim-
pled elbow. The adjectives of ordinary advertise-
ment fail to exaggerate the success of Rickards's
latest importation from ’Ome. Will Crackles
takes the position of chief luminary in the new
comet’s taiL

Pretty Mrs. Potter and Beautiful Bellew
Are once again on view.
Their tricky airs and graces,
4nd interesting faces,
Seem quite as good as new.
Welcome ! Potter and Bellew.
Many years of absence, or, certainly, a few
(Good gracious ! how they flew !),
Have touched the classic features
Of those ever-blooming creatures
Refreshingly as dew
Fell on buttercups that grew.
Prettv Mrs. Potter and Beautiful Bellew
Tis a pleasure to review.
Six intervening summers,
Since last we saw those mummers,
Have faded out of view ;
The old time is the new,
And vain appears our weeping
For some dear ones who are sleeping
’Neath the shadow of the yew.
Buried objects of affection
Owe a seeming resurrection
To Potter and Bellew.
And the cud of false reflection
We can chew.
At Poverty Point.

The words of “ Ben Bolt.” English as they seem, were
written by an American, Thomas Dunn English, who, in
’94, was still living in a town in New Jersey. In the
summer of that year, when “Trilby” was all the vogue,
Mr. English was dined by the Lotos Club in New York,
and he then gave his hosts a gossipy little history of
“ Ben Bolt,” written more than half -a -century before.
The air to which it is sung is the composition of an Eng-
lishman. Mr. English, if alive, is now in his 77th year.

Any number of ambitious diving professors are anxious
to try their luck in Peart's fatal tank. The apparent im-
possibility of putting any new diver to the test, without
letting him rush into grave danger, is the point to be con-
sidered. Peart came to the Fitzgeralds with a reputa-
tion. He had performed his feat thousands of times, and
was so reliable that they would have insured his life for
£lOOO, at a very small advance on the ordinary premium.

The recent arrest of Henry Butler, in
Melbourne, on various charges, recalls to memory
a rather famous murder of 16 years ago.
Butler, who has received sentences aggregating
40 years since 1860, is commonly alleged to have
gone a-burgling in the house of one Dewar in
Cumberland-street, Dunedin, and to have but-
chered the entire family with an axe, and then set
the premises on fire. The legend further has it
that Mrs. Dewar, when a romantic school-girl in
a Northern town, had become secretly engaged to
Butler, then a young and good-looking man, but
he got into gaol, and when he came out his
fiancee was married and gone, and thereupon (so
went the police theory) he pursued her with the
axe aforementioned and demolished her and her
family by way of vengeance. The evidence was
strong, but Butler conducted his own defence with
remarkable skill J and 12 jurymen, all with their
hair parted in the middle, gave him the benefit of
the doubt. Butler had previously challenged
every juryman whose hair wasn't parted in the
middle, and when the trial was over he candidly
explained his reasons. “It is safest,” he said,
“to get a jury of weak-minded men. Every man
who parts his hair in the middle is more or less
weak-minded.” A few days later he was tried
again—rhis time for burgling the house of a lawyer
with firearms, and other aggravating circum-
stances. The second jury didn’t wear its hair
sepiarated in the middle, and the result was a
sentence of IS years. Butler, by the way, is a
State-made criminal. Before he was 12 he was
arrested for ragronc* and received a stiff sentence.
Shortly after Ins release he was again arrested on
the same charge, and sent up for a longer term.
When this latter term had been served the youth
knew as much as the toughs with whom he had
associated in gaol could teach him, and on his
release he began a career of crime that has made
two provinces sweat gold.

The Wild-Cat Column.
Clifton Subscriber : (1) Can’t get any reliable
Information as to how things are turning out, but they
look very bad. (2) The Illawarra Mutual Building Society
looks perfectly sound and prosperous. There is nothing
on the face of the balance-sheet to create the slightest
suspicion.
Among the claims against the City of Melbourne
Bank is one of £31,720 by the Commissioners of Melbourne
Savings Bank. In 1891 the Savings Bank made one of its
big disastrous loans to Wallachand Fink, when it advanced
£40,000 on mortgage at per cent. Apparently, how-
ever, the Commissioners felt none too certain about the
security, so it was stipulated that the City of Melbourne
Bank should guarantee payment of the interest in per-
petuity. Then Wallach and Fink went down in the
financial storm. The security depreciated to £25,000, or
£16,000 less than the loan and arrears of interest, and the
guaranteeing bank burst. So the Commissioners claim
from the liquidators of the C.O.M. £31,720 as being the
capitalised value of 7£ per cent, interest on £16,000 from
now to the end of time ; and the Supreme Court has gone
into a cool spot to think it over. The question naturally
arises whether it wouldn’t be much simpler for the
C.O.M. Bank to pay the Savings Bank the £16,000
capital it has lost rather than leave the £16,000
outstanding and pay £31,720 interest on it, but
apparently this isn’t in the bill. The Colonial Mutual
Insurance Society has an exactly similar case on
hand. It lent £3500 on land now* worth only £922,
and the interest was guaranteed by the Mercan-
tile Finance Trustees and Agency Co. It also lent
£4695 on land now worth only £3753, with the same
guarantee as regards the interest. Then the se-
curity and the borrowers and the guaranteeing co. all
came to grief together ; and the Colonial Mutual sues the
liquidators of the Mercantile Finance, &c. co. for
authority to rank as a creditor for £6804, the capitalised
value of future interest, as well as for £2165 interest in
arrears. The law is thinking hard over this also, and the
whole affair is a fine sample of how they financed in the
Melbourne boom time. Obviously the Bent and Fink
variety of borrowers were viewed with some suspicion,
and apparently the securities were also viewed with some
suspicion else the guarantee wouldn’t have been sug-
gested, and if the banks which did that kind of business
weren’t viewed with suspicion they certainly should have
been. So the three suspicions got together and guaran-
teed each other, and all was supposed to be well.
* * * *
One good feature about the N.S.W. Closer
Settlement Bill is that the money to pay for lands
acquired for settlement is to be borrowed locally. As
nothing is done in this Great Loan Land without borrow-
ing it is at least satisfactory that the borrowing is to be
done in the least disastrous fashion possible. But a much
better device would be simply to enact that the seller
shall be paid with 3 per cent, stock issued at the price
then current in the local market, the stock to have a cur-
rency of, say, 20 years, and thereafter to be redeemable
solely at the option of the Government. There would be
no hardship to the seller of land ; he would get his
securities, not at their nominal value but at the value
they would fetch in the market, so that they would be
equivalent to cash. And this system would save the
State borrowing money before it was wanted and paying
interest on it while it lay idle, and also save the loss that
arises through borrowing £300,000 to pay for a £227,000
purchase and leaving the balance unproductive, possibly
for a year or more. Further, it would save expenses and
commissions, which all amount to something.
* » * *
That cheeky and aggressive concern, the British
Committee of Foreign Bond-holders, attempts again to
blackmail the Maoriland Government into guaranteeing
the New Plymouth Harbor Board debentures by the
threat that it will conspire to wreck any future M.L.
loans if something isn’t done. Said Board, in the old
insane days, floated a big loan in London at the awful rate
of 6 per cent. Despite many difficulties, it has paid up
everything save 6s. per cent., which is a month or two in
arrear and will be liquidated shortly ; and despite this
temporary inconvenience the £lOO bonds are saleable at
£lO5, so the holders are doing remarkably well. Yet the
unspeakable Bull isn’t satisfied, and wants a State guar-
antee or something of the kind, which wasn’t in the bond,
in addition to his 6 per cent., and talks of raising sheol if
he doesn’t get it.
* * * *
Lambton seems to be the champion fool munici-
pality of N.S.W. In 1890 it was decided to borrow £7OOO
for electric-lighting purposes, and the money was raised
from the Commercial Bank of Australia at 7 per cent,.,
repayable on demand. As the total income of the muni-
cipality from all sources was only about £lBOO a year, and
the expenses, including interest, were over £2200, of
course it was quite obvious that the money couldn’t be
paid on demand, and that when the demand came the
miserable township would crumple up. In order to avoid
being crumpled it has now been fighting the case for two
years in the law courts on a technical point. If it loses
in the end the debt and the 7 per cent, and the law-costs
will about eat up Lambton.
* * * *
One evidence in favor of the Maoriland State
Loan Department is that, out of the £700,000 which it
has already advanced at 5 per cent., about £500,000 went
to pay off old mortgages at rates varying from 7 to 15
per cent. Consequently, on this half-million alone, the
saving to the borrowers should be somewhere about
£12,000 a year. And meanwhile Robert Stout, K.C.M.G.
(whose wife publicly believes in the importation of China-
men) condemns the proposed anti-usury legislation of the
Seddon Government on the ground that all attempts at
legal interference with the rate of interest are bound to
be a failure. The M.L. State Loan Department certainly
looks like a species of legal interference.
There are now only five N.S. Wales fire and
marine insurance companies surviving; eight years ago
there were eleven. And there are more than 40 foreign
offices (i.e., offices which do not belong to Australia,
Maoriland, or Tasmania) doing business in the province,
among them English, Scotch, German, Austrian, Chinese,
and others. According to the last available returns, at
Dec., ’94, Sydney and suburbs were insured for about
£60,000,000, and of this the local offices did only
£24,000,000, or just two-fifths. In the same eight years
the Vic. offices have diminished from nine to seven ; the
South Australian from four to one ; and the Maoriland
from seven to five. These reflections are aroused by the
recent arrival of another British office in N.S.W. When
is the N.S.W. Protectionist party going to move all it is
worth for a heavy tax on absentee fire-insurance offices ?
There is no local industry which is more in want
of Protection than the fire-insurance business, and if
such a move can’t be made now, it certainly should
be one of the first things to be attended to when
Federation comes along. Almost every other kind
of foreign competition has been taxed at one time or
other, but the foreign insurance co. has always gone free,
even in Maoriland, which taxes the extraneous bagman
£5O per annum. The only difference that has been made
so far is that when the N.S.W. insurance co. puts its
money into Government securities it has to pay income-
tax on the interest, while the foreign one doesn’t.

Chaffey Bros.’ workmen at Mildura haven’t
been paid regularly since 1891, and some of the arrears of
wages extend four years back. Even the customs duty
on the pumping-plant is unpaid ; the ’Murkan speculators
were allowed to erect the plant in bond and owe the duty,
and it is owing to this day.
Tf Loo iiiof Boon diaonvorod that Rem l lark. the
Chaffey Bros.’ S.A. irrigation settlement, was secretly
mortgaged, in 1891, for £lOO,OOO, to the British debenture-
holders. The mortgage wasn’t registered, and was kept
so dark that its existence has just been discovered ; and
this, coming on top of the other liabilities, makes things
look worse than ever for the local creditors. The Chaf-
fey 8 ’ capacity for pawning and borrowing seems to have
been almost unlimited.
* * * *
The curse of muddlement and mismanagement
seems still to hang over Mildura. When the Chaffey co.
exploded, the Vic. Government guaranteed an advance of
£3500 to keep the settlement afloat pending more definite
arrangements, and the trustees thereupon borrowed the
money from a bank at 6£ per cent., making the interest
£227 10s. per annum. The State could have got the
money at 3£ per cent, and lent it direct, and its liability
would have been no greater than it is, while the chances
of repayment would have been a lot better. The per
cent, curse has been on Mildura from its first inception.
On the Vic. railways <£20,000 a year is now
sa,ved, as compared with the Speight era, by the use of
Gippsland coal in place of Newcastle ; £28,000 a year is
saved on lubricants, principally by the use of mineral
instead of vegetable oils ; and another economy of £2OOO
a year is being effected by using fire-kindlers to light the
engine fires instead of wood as heretofore. Economy is
down to a tolerably fine point in the Victorian Govt,
departments nowadays.

The Mercantile Bank liquidator reports that
the losses already written off are £781,000, and out of the
£647,000 still due to the bank by debtors about £530,000
will probably prove hopeless. Under these conditions it
will probably take a hard struggle to raise the dividend to
10s. in the £.
» « » »
Liquidator Ducker, who now speaks with re-
spect of the men he used to snort at, was asked to
explain to Mercantile Bank shareholders why some of
them should be beggared by paying calls, whereas others,
who haven’t paid their calls, are wallowing in luxury?
The question was directed, firstly, at certificated boom-
sters, and secondly, at the courts of justice which relieved
them from their debts. Ducker’s answer was a petulant
remark that he didn’t administer laws, or something to
the same effect. The question remains for such as
Matthew Davies and Chief Justice Madden to answer.
Can his Honor show good reason for the present apparent
opulence of the relieved boomsters in the face of other
people’s compulsory cashing up ? There is only a legal
reason for the exasperating prosperity of Smellbourne’s
whitewashed Fat Men. One of these days a crazy share-
holder will arm himself with a meat-axe and lay a few
lawyers out.

Cabled that the S.A. Company, an aggregation
of English absentees, has paid a div. of £1 16s. per share,
the profit for the year being £24,000. This is the co.
founded by Geo. Fife Angas, the pious philanthropist, in
the interests of the poor emigrant, in order “to keep
out of the reach of those who are ever ready to take
the fruits of the labor, prudence and skill of the indivi-
duals whose future lot may be cast on the south shores of
Australia, and to let industry have its fair chance of ob-
taining reward.” Geo. Fife Angas, then a poor man, died
a millionaire, and the S.A. co. has the unenviable reputa-
tion of being a very bowelless institution indeed.
* * * *
An important point in winding-up law was
raised in Melb. the other day on a motion for final judg-
ment for calls due as a contributory on the “B ” list. It
was contended that the original holder of the shares, the
“ B” list man, could not be called on to pay until it was
shown that his transferree on the “A” list had first been
exhausted. There’s a good deal of legal confusion between
the liability of being placed on the lists of contributories,
and the individual liabilities of the “ A ” and “ B ” people
when “settled.” As the books appear to be silent on
this subject, and as the case is one which crops up every
day, an interesting decision is expected.
Newcastle strike is fizzling out, after having
caused a lot of loss 10 owners, port, and men. Appar-
ently the only people to profit by It are the South Coast
and Victorian colliers. The Bulli and Wollongong coal
output for May was 77,783 tons—the highest on record.
So far as Newcastle is concerned, the issue might have
been foreseen from the beginning.
* * * ♦
Q. Labor-leader Glassey’s piano has possibly
caused more noise than a piano ever caused before. A
BriBb. piano-firm sold this celebrated instrument on time-
payment, and the purchaser re-sold to Glassey for spot
cash before he had fully paid up his instalments. Then
the piano-firm sued Glassey for cash or piano, and Glassey
won, but lost on appeal to the Full Court, and has now to
return the instrument and pay all costs. The judgment
emphasises the principle that the time-payment purchaser
has no title till he has paid the last stiver.

That noble vessel the Brightside, alias Emu,
sank the other day at anchor in Sydney harbor. People
who have had to cross the Heads on a rough morning in
the Emu will be delighted to hear that she is being raised.

• * * *
Not many Australian Judges would exhibit
either the inclination or the courage of Boucaut (S.A.)
the other day, when he advised a bank that was being
sued to come to a “settlement at once and save the
expenses,” because it was clearly in the wrong. It was a
big and strong bank, too. *
A red-hot N.S.W. bankrupt applied for his cer-
tificate the other day. He had first given a bill-of-sale
over all his assets, then contracted the debts as to which
he bankrupted, and then dishonored a few hundreds’
worth of p.n.’s, besides having been “bankrupt a few
years ago, when his estate didn’t pay any div.” And only
a suspension of 18 months ! Cheap at the price.
The French Government has instructed its Resi-
dent in Madagascar to refuse prospecting - licenses
for mining in the protectorate to all except French citi-
zens. Some Australians, it appears, had applied for
licenses, and accordingly warning to the above effect has
been sent to the Australian Governments.
The P. and O. Co. have in hand two vessels
specially-constructed for the Anglo-Australian live-cattle
trade.
* * * *
England is advancing rapidly to Protection.
The Government, in order to help the starving cattle-
raiser in an underhand way, is introducing a bill making
the slaughter of imported cattle compulsory at the port
of landing.

Mt. Drysdale, N.S.W., has struck some more
rich rock, and the shares have moved upwards. One
mine, one boom, and this show has had it. There are too
many who bought at from £5OO to £750 a share waiting to

* * * *
Sydney D.T., in arguing that a State coal-mine
would further depress the wages of coal-hewers, chiefly
forgets the prominent fact that the State colliery would
not need to pay dividends out of the wages of its colliers.
* * * •
Australasia has at last got another mining boom
in gold, silver, and copper, with occasional spurts in
diamonds, platinum, and other metals and gems. None
can tell the stage of the boom —whether it is the begin-
ning, the middle, or near the end. All booms are born to
die suddenly—they never linger. Of one thing there is no
doubt —the boom is here. Another is that the public are
coming along like lambs to be slaughtered. Old silver
mines, abandoned when silver was nearly double its pre-
sent price, are resurrected under the influence of the new
sulphide-processes. In the absence of new gold-mines old
ones are being re-opened, ostensibly for work, but really
to work the public. Shares that this day next year will not
be worth a penny a dozen are now at a big premium. The
great mining tipster, after a long sleep and silence over
Bonanza Consols and 50 other now-liquidated mines reli-
giously tipped by him, has arisen in his might, shaken off
the filth of the old boom, and now in the new says, “ I
told you so,” tipping nearly everything on the market,
especially every new thing issued.
* * * *
Out of the 96720 z. gold alleged to be produced
at Coolgardie in April, the Great Boulder, Lake View, and
Ivanhoe mines—none of which are at Coolgardie—yielded
71340 z. A few other mines, none of which are at Cool-
gardie either, made up the balance. Coolgardie itself
now looks about as good a goldfield as Woolloomooloo.
* » * *
God has been good to Coolgardie country.
Born in Bayley’s Reward, nursed by the Plunderderry,
fostered by the Wealth of Expectations, endowed with
immortality by the Great Boulder, it is now supposed to
have received its crown of glory in a,rich find of telluride
of gold. Assayer Holroyd is the hero of the allegedly
extensive telluride discovery made in Block 45 at
Hannan’s. And several other leases are said to have since
found it. According to the wires every assayer now
seems to be looking out for telluride.
A few weeks ago the papers gave the public to
believe that there was a year’s supply of surface water in
the lakes at Hannan’s and elsewhere on the fields. There
were “ boating parties on the lakes,” which were covered
with game, etc., and now comes : “Kalgoorlie. Thursday.
—Owing to the recent hot weather the water on the sur-
face of Hannan’s lake has disappeared in a very short
time, but an immense quantity is stored in the bed of the
lake. The lake is like a big sponge full of water.” But
they take care not to say that the water when it gets into
that sponge has to be got out at great expense in digging
channels, sinking shafts, and pumping ; that it can only
be used as it is for crushing—for every other purpose it
has to be condensed, and, being densely salt, that is an
expensive necessity. The water-difficulty is the everlast-
ing curse of the Coolgardie fields.

The Bulletin hears that two Roebourne (W.A.)
claims the Edith and Castle have been floated for
£140,000, an absurdly high sum, although the stone is
promising. The Yellow Aster and Golden King, on which
Calvert paid a deposit on his visit, have been hung in the
wind for some time, but Capt. Symons has reported
favorably on them, and they are likely to go off.

London man, to whom he had promised a good mine, and
who after the Boulder was floated went to him and reviled
him for not having kept his promise, “ Hold hard, I’ll
give you one worth perhaps three Boulders ! ” And he sold
him the Lady Loch.

The battery-manager was having a slanging-
match with the boss of a small party, the members of
which had just cleaned up, for next-to-nothing a load, a.
crushing of stone that prospected something like 23dwt.
“ I tell you ! ” yelled the battery-man, “ this is as good a
mill as there is in Australia, and saves every weight. Ain’t
we got all the gold-savin’ appliances?” “Gold-savin’
appliances ! ” shrieked the other, “ gold-savin’ appliances !
Why, damn me ! there is more gold in the tailin’s than
there is in the stuff afore it goes through your infernal
mill ! ”

Koch’s Pioneer, a coming Bendigo crack, had
spelled for 15 years till it was re-worked in ’95. Shaft
then stood at 1365 ft. and the co. had barely got 50ft.
further before it struck the mass of spurs now breaking
over the 1450 ft. level—ssft. high, averaging 15ft. thick,
and opened up for 300 ft. This run has milled 2512 tons
for 17560 z., paying several divs. Koch’s P., bought in ’7l
for £75,000, and has called up £18,350 and paid £27,000
divs.

burra and Strzlecki district showing 26,814,824 tons ; Jum-
bunna and Outrun, 35,755,511 tons, and half-a-dozen
smaller localities contributing the balance. Value of out-
put to date, £381,216.
* * ♦ *
Vic. Mining on Private Property Act provides
that in the event of disputes between the owner and the
miner applying for a lease as to the amount of compensa-
tion for surface-damage, the matter is to be referred to
the Warden. The Chief Justice has just decided that,
although one section of the Act was doubtful on the
point, the intention of the Legislature was that these pro-
visions should apply to a miner working under a miner’s
right as well as to the applicant for a lease. The case was
from the Lilydale district, and is one of many recent
indications that mining is looking up again on the Wandin
and at the old Emerald diggings. The surface-damage
claims, often exorbitant, are the principal handicaps to
this otherwise promising fossicking field.

* * * *
Windmill Hill, one of Bendigo’s boss yielders,
has a record. Five years’ developing turned out two
divs. in the early part of ’7O, when the gold suddenly
streaked off, and shilling “ heartbreakers ” had the
innings till ’94. Then pluck had a ldok in, the co. at the
1310 ft. cutting a big reef, and another 100 ft. landed it on
another massive formation —to date opened by 460 ft. in
length and with another 400 ft. yet to tackle. These
makes have milled 15,4100 z. from 19,254 loads, and div’d
£25,000 on the £42,500 called up. Windmill illustrates
reefer Lansell’s pertinacity. An original scripper, his
faith in the mine never wavered ; he snapped all the “ for-
feiteds ” that offered, carried the venture through its lean
years, and now owns 18,000 out of 82,000 shares to draw
urofits from. A man of grit.
The Frederick the Great at last means to thrill
its British owners, who have dumped over £20,000 into it,
not to mention the “ splosh” that went to the promoters.
Between the 580 ft. and 880 ft. levels now breaking a reef
proved 200 ft. high and 40ft. wide, gold well distributed,
and looks payable. Stone easily won, and, bashed by
60 head of stamps, about 3dwt. will meet ex’s.
» * * *
Victoria quartz, on the New Chum line, makes
Bendigo’s deep-level beauty trio. Raising soz. from
2554 ft. By the strike of the winze it is reckoned the reef
cap is 46ft. above the saddle, and a 10ft. thickness of stone
is being taken out without either wall visible. Co. has a
gorgeous prospect, and,- to feel its full strength, will sink
its shaft another lift and erect a new winding-plant. Vie.
scrip is well held, very little of it figuring on the market.

Red, White and Blue, a Bendigo side-line mine,
has div’d £116,000 on £4500 capital. From this it is
argued the Bendigo Development Co. to test the side-
lines, which started shaft-sinking the other day, has
a good chance. Success of this venture would open up a
large area of likely country.

* * * *
A Southern syndicate has had an expert at the
last collapsed goldfield spying out “ likely things,” and
consequently parties have their paddocks baited for jays.
Specimens were in strong demand for a week or two, but
the specialist was more expert than was expected, andhas
not taken the hook. Claims that were offered at £5
suddenly sprang up to £l5O, and two or three parties
helped each other to gild their little heaps by a generous
exchange of specimens to crown the tips. The expert
recognised the same specimen on three tips quite half-a-
mile apart.
* * * *
Ballarat as a mining centre isn’t a patch on
Bendigo for liveness. Directorate cliqueism, the slowness
of legal managers, and a general mystifying air of “don’t
know ” combine to sit heavily on the aforesaid field. For
these reasons the public doesn’t gamble in Ballarat stocks.
As to the legal managers, they are mostly mere dummies,
and never seem to know how their mines are jumping-
all the time the inside crowd and its Melb. tail have
cleaned the market on some good development. Also, at
Ballarat several “ boss” directors daily visit the principal
mines and browbeat the miners to get information. Thus
armed, it is easy to “ ring a scoop ” on shareholders
beyond the pale of early news.
* * * *
A dead-beat traveller in the vicinity of Tongu
West (Vic.) the other day picked up a rock to throw at his
dog, then changed his mind and put it in his pocket. It
had 2£oz. clean gold hanging to it. A thing like this
doesn’t always turn out for the best, though. A school-
mate of the present writer once found a handsome speci-
men on the road at Soldiers’ Hill, Ballarat, and up to date
that young man has wasted about seven years of,.his life
looking over stones for more nuggets without striking a
color.
* * * *
Vic. Amalgamated Miners are pulling the ropes
for a reform of the existing “ tribit” system, under which
the working miner is too often entirely at the mercy of
the fatted director and frequently finds himself utterly
“ out of it ” when he strikes good stone after a hard
struggle amongst bread-and-dripping stuff. Mines Foster
promises profusely. He is the most “ promising ” man
they have ever had in the Department.

£1,586,758 9s. 7d. ; divs., £848,700, or £47 3s. per share ;

another div. or two in the plant and tailings. By the
death of Madame, the laughing Peacock, legal manager,

“ toffs,” Madame Berry West and Berry Consols, in his
office.

A variant of the old story of the goose with the
nuggets in its crop. A farmer in Haddon-raad, BHlarat,
Vic., finding that his horse was walking lame, got off his
waggon, and, lifting the animal’s hoof, found a piece of
quartz with 2oz. gold attached embedded in the frog.

Lady Augusta, Eidsvold, Q., cleaned up 38 tons
for 2720 z. gold—7oz. 3dwts. per ton. For the five months
of this year 86 tons have been crushed for 4860 z. gold—an
average of soz. 13dwts. The reef runs about 6in., and the
country rock is hard, black granite. Only a few men are
employed. The Mt. Rose and Stockman Junction, which
paid £34,000 in divs., but has been sick for the last two
years, is now working a 2ft. reef of alleged ounce stone ;
divs. are again dreamt of.
» * # *
Never joke on money-matters. The other day,
near Herberton, Brown commissioned, by way of joke,
his friend Robinson to buy mining shares to the value
of £l2OO, with commission of 21%. Robinson immediately
departed, bought the whole mine for £l2OO, and paid £69
12s. as deposit. Brown was simply astounded when he
heard the news, refused to go on with the sale and for-
feited his deposit. Robinson then sued for deposit, £25
commission, and coach-fare—total, £105.. Verdict for £69
12s. and coach-fare—each side paying its own costs.
» * * *
The Adelaide brokers are working at fever-heat.
Nowadays, Sydney brokers work mainly for the Melbourne
and Adelaide markets. At one time, Adelaide was slow
enough, in a mining sense, but it got drunk at Broken
Hill time, and has never been quite sober since.

TO A DIRTY OURRENCY.
Oh dirty, malodorous shreds of pollution !
Foul pasture for micro-bacterial motes,
To blazes I’ll send you for fiery ablution
Could I only afford it —vile-smelling bank-notes !
While I loathe you, I bless you, and fervently pray
(Altho’ your aroma and touch I abhor)
That my purse, now so empty, you’ll bulge every day,
And may I impound you by thousands galore 1
Mean, flimsy presentments of gold’s solid worth,
I detest you and love you in one puny breath ;
Still quite as presentable, now, as at birth,
I’ll scorn, yet embrace, till the hour of my death !
W.S.W.

It has been asserted that the Kanaka is
incapable of being properly civilised. Not only
can the Kanaka be civilised, but he clings passion-
ately to the usages of cultured people—cherishing
even the impedimenta the former curse so heartily.
A wild Kanaka was recently found in the Isis
Scrub (Q.) perfectly naked but for a paper-collar.
* * * *
The ladies—bless their hearts ! of the
Sydney unemployed who deputated Premier Reid
the other day, asking him to find work for their
men folk, thus perorated in their written petition :
We hope that you (Reid) will have a long and happy
Reign (!), and live long, and meet with a happy death (!).
And be an archangel in the sky foreverandever-
amen ! Why not complete the forecast ?
* * * *
“Can’t you women take in washing?”
said jolly-and-fat Premier Reid to the deputation
of Sydney women with unemployed husbands and
starving families. “No. The Chinamen and
blackfellows get all the washing,” was promptly
chorused. “ I’ve had 15 children. Seven of ’em
are alive (here’s a fact for Dr. Muskett). One is
under 12 months old, and my husband is at home
minding it, in order that I may come here,” said
another. Oh, yes ; nursing mothers, with litters
of children, to go out, or take in, washing. The
babes and sucklings would help them at it.

A novel idea was recently introduced into
tennis at Mackay (Q.). At the close of the game
the gentlemen were invited to award the prize of
beauty to the most attractive player of the other
sex—which they accordingly did. In return the
ladies ballotted for the most agreeable gentleman
present, whom they promptly invested with a
little gold pm neatly carven into a donkey’s head,
ihe emblem is singularly appropriate for two-
thirds of the alleged lady-killers in all countries.

* * * *
N.S.W. State-school head-masters and
mistresses are in future to be remunerated accord-
ing to the grade of their schools; heretofore they
have been paid according to the certificate held.
But the Education authorities found that, under
this system of payment, some 1A certificated
teachers in charge of low-grade schools were en-
joying salaries intended only to be paid in the case
of first-class schools. Hence the new by-law,
which has saved the department some ,£3OOO since
the beginning of the year.
* * * *
Tooth : “I like the woman who
reads novelettes. I don’t like the one
who reads history, science, newspapers,
theology, botany, microscopic human
anatomy, or any other surgery. The
novelettiste is like a nice glove. She
knows all that is worth knowing. How
nattily she dresses, and hangs out the
angle for the nobleman sure to come
along.”
* * *
The Daily Telegraph, Launces-
ton (Tas.), a very, very moral paper
(which steals and reprints Bulletin mat-
ter without acknowledgment under a
heading called “In a Lighter Vein”),
published a column or more of matter
re the suiciding of Mrs. Edith Price, of
Melbourne. Every step of the journey,
from the room where the deed was done
to the looking-glass in which the poor
victim viewed her wounds, was traced
with ghastly exactness ; and every streak
and drop of blood was noted by the
gloating eye of the gory-minded scribe ;
all this for the benefit, of the unfor-
tunate lady’s relatives, who belonged
to Launceston.
* * *
The uniform in which Alfred
Deakin, the Radical, dazzled all eyes at
Gov. Brassey’s Birthday levee, was that
of the third-class of the Civil Service,
which, by the Queen’s regulations, a
colonial Executive Councillor is entitled
to wear. The suit is a mass of real gold-
lace and buttons, and costs about <£6o.

A cripple was brought before a
Sydney P.M. the other day, charged
with having no lawful visible means of
support. “What?” said the P.M.,
looking at the poor fellow’s crutches;
“ nonsense ! ’Smissed ! ”
It has transpired that it has been the
vogue of “ the ladies ” of the Sydney branch of
the “ Royal ” Society for the Prevention of Cruelty
to Animals to give their inspectors a number of
blank summons forms. One joker, now in gaol
for two years, used to get a J.P.’s name on to each
summons, fill in the name of a possible defendant,
pretend the feat had cost him ss. or 65., and black-
mail victims by offering to withdraw the summons
on payment of costs. . . . Which reminds us of a
Brisbane lady who, having to call together her
creditors, announced that she had bought land,
built a house, and purchased furniture on three’
six, and nine months’ bills; she didn’t know for
how much; she had just given her business manager
a number of blank p.n.’s, and he had done as he
chose with them !

“DOUBTS DYING.”
Time! take from us
In our last hours,
Faith, Hope’s promise—
They’re withered flowers !
“ Do not flout them,”
Time sadly saith,
“For without them,
What sop to death ! ”
TRILBY
FOR THE BULLETIN
-f r r/AAH on/
K K.
E read in tales of olden
time
(As gospel we receive it)
How devilsentered into
swine
(Of course, we all believe it).
We’re all aware that Bible-talk
Unvarnished truth of course is,
Eor Hebrew devils dwell in pork,
And Christian ones—in horses.
The Devil himself was in her eye,
And fifty imps inside her ;
The Devil alone was game to try
To rope her in and ride her.
She was a filly, full of sport,
As equine youngsters will be ;
She acted as a filly ought
Whose maiden name is Trilby.
And now she’s dead, and gone to where
Four-legged devils rest—
A fitting ending for the mare
I owned (and Nick possessed);
For though Tareela riders are
Artists, and ever will be,
’Twas no disgrace for them to bar
“ That little demon —Trilby.”
RACKAROCK.
AT HER GATE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How blest the wandered bird that sings
With such a woodland ecstasy
Till song is sorrow’s self, and he
Holds on thy roof his fretted wings,
All pain forgotten when with thee !
Thus would my wandered heart achieve,
So far outborne on wayward tide,
A still roof in thy heart, to hide
Sheltered from lonely night, and weave
Youth’s dream again, and there abide !
M aonland.
H.C.

The name of “ E. Dyson ” at the foot of a
yarn or a bit of verse is familiar to all readers of
The Bulletin, and is generally accepted as a
guarantee that something well worth study lies
above it. Mr. Dyson’s book of verse, entitled
“ Rhymes from the Mines and Other Lines,” is to
be published in style by Angus and Robertson
next month, and will be supplied direct from this
office.
One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respect
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*

hazardous force. —A gentleman, who is now above

force. It acts according to the quantity taken—
either as a relieving medicine, or as a cooling and
refreshing drink : and I am convinced that it does
not weaken when it stimulates.” Caution
Examine each bottle, and see the capsule is
marked “ Eno’s Fruit Salt.” Without it, you
have only a worthless imitation. Sold by all
chemists and stores.*

Tasmania has set up a female
Oddfellows’ Lodge under the title
“The Loyal Maids of Cornwall.”
Is the married she-Oddfellow ex-
cluded, then, or is the term
“ maid ” supposed to have no more
significance than in the case of
“barmaid”? For was it not re-
counted in court that on a certain
historic occasion in a Melb. subur-
ban pub., a customer called “the
barmaid,” and the “ girl ” behind
the bar replied, hotly : “ Excuse
me, sir ! I am a married woman.’
* * *
Melbourne is now about the
most canvasser - cursed city on
earth. Every trade and profession
has its representative, else does
the principal quickly migrate or
come to inhabit any accidentally-
empty tank on the adjacent wharf.
The ordinary Smellbournite, a
couple of years ago, could easily
paper any ordinary room with
summonses and writs served on
him, say, in six months ; now, he
can readily shingle a ten-roomed
villa with the pasteboards of
people willing to feed, clothe, drug
or bury him, received during the
same period. The two best-paying
Melbourne businesses at present
should be the printing-offices and
the cardboard-mill at Broadford.
* * *
A lecturess at a Tas. Wo-
man-Suffrage meeting, held under
the auspices of the W.C.T. U.,
waxed eloquent when quoting
Huxley in support of the woman
vote. The assemblage preserved
an ominous silence. Then the
lady-talker quoted Parkes on
women, and the audience clapped
its large, damp hands in hysterical
joy and stamped its hoofs in un-
qualified approval. Great Spirit
of Humbug, live for ever !
* * *
Passengers on a suburban-
line station, a few days ago, were
pleased to hear the station-master
informing a brother official, per
telephone, that he had lost his
railway-pass owing to his wife’s
aunt having her pocket picked at
a station some hundreds of miles
inland. The aunt was probably
taking the pass for repairs.

In the recent S. Melb. election the united
council of the Womanhood-suffrage Association-
including such lights as Mrs. Bear Crawford, Miss
Michie (daughter of the Q.C.), and the Misses
Goldstein —canvassed the district in the interests
of H. H. Champion on bicycles. They were
assured that Mr. Champion would get the support
of the electors if he could explain certain things—
including the strike, and open letters addressed to
Dr. Bevan and to Michael Davitt. Mr. Cham-
pion’s champions were a plucky set: Miss Michie,
Miss LILLA WILDE (Her Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney).
Photo, by Falk.
it is said, actually waited upon attorney O’Hal-
loran, of Dowry case fame, and asked his vote for
H.H.C. The owner of the filly was painfully
silent, and would give no promises.
* * * *
There turned up at Sydney Divorce Court
last week a couple who had married four years
ago, when He was 70 and She was 06 years old.
Moreover, one of His daughters had previously
married one of Her sons. Yet the course of
this family true love ran riotously. He used to
lock Her out after 10 p.m. and throttle Her and
threaten strangulation. She, it was averred, used
to belt Him with a stick and had blinded one of
His eyes with a stroke. She now petitioned for
judicial separation and—got it. Is marriage a
failure ?
King O’Malley, who has entered the S.A.
Leg. Assembly with the prime object of abolish-
ing barmaids, expects the support of at least four
of the Ministers, the whole of the Labor-
party, and two or three teetotal Conservatives,
lie will probably not propose the immediate
sweeping away of the system, but that no places
vacated by a girl shall be filled by a girl, and
that no barmaid once out of a position shall re-
engage at an hotel.
Chief - Justice Maddem
vexed by the difficulty o f
making witnesses speak up>
remarked that, “ really there
seems a danger of Australia
being damned with small
voices.” Lecturer Charles
Clark, who can make a whis-
per heard in the farthest
corner of the largest hall, so
well-trained is his voice, read
this observation, and his com-
ment was the happy quota-
tion of the lines from Words-
worth’s “ Peter Bell ” :
Some sipping punch, some drink-
ing tea,
But, as you by their faces see,
All silent and all damned.
* * *
The thing that strikes
Charles Clark most on his re-
turn to Melbourne, where he
once pastorised, is the debt-
worriedness of the churches.
When he was minister of Al-
bert-street church he left it
free from debt, and with a
large congregation. Now its
condition is precisely other-
wise. A stone-throw away is
Chalmers church, which is so
hard-up that the parsonage has
had to be let, and there is talk
of selling the whole place. Just
at the back, the Unitarian
church is a mere shell —hardly
anyone attends it except Gyles
Turner, the Commercial Bank
manager, who practically keeps
it going.

A Melb. pressman is endeavoring to make
a living by teaching ladies to play chess. He
ought to be wildly successful if he can teach them
anything new in the way of mating.
“BEN BOLT” UP TO DATE.
0, don’t you find women much altered, Ben Bolt,
Since Alice was put in the ground ?
She would wink nowadays, if you gave her a smile,
And turn up her nose if you frowned.
She would travel about with a bike, Ben Bolt,
And talk in a truculent tone ;
Let us sit on her slab, and be happy to say
Sweet Alice lies under the stone —
Smoke a pipe on the slab, and be ha-a-ppy to say
Sweet Alice lies under the stone.
One is told to think well of the sex, Ben Bolt,
They are tender and true, more or less ;
But where is the man who could give up the ghost
For a “ tart ” in a rational dress?
You have got a New Woman at home, Ben Bolt,
As you sometimes remark with a moan ;
Sweet Alice, who might have been living to-day,
Was better laid under the stone :
She’s a vision of youth, which can never decay
Although she lies under the stone.
E. F.
A THOUGHT.
[For The Bulletin.]
As Night fell black from the womb of Space
A man stared moodily in my face,
And a woman was weeping and wringing her
hands,
And a little child was calling.
And I thought, in Civilisation’s night
How many sullen eyes seek light !
How many women are wringing their hands .
How many children calling !

Sydney, Juno 9, 1890.
Mr Dear Moorabinda —
The committee-lady who idly complained to one
of her friends, the other evening, that “ nothing
funny ever happens at a public ball,” had every
reason to withdraw her complaint at the Fresh
Air League Ball, held in Sydney Town Hall.
Some mad wag had arranged the obsolete
quadrilles in place of the conventional lancers
for the first V.R. official set, and when the
salt of the gathering, 16 strong, took the floor
with the public eye upon them their confusion was
absolute. Nobody knew precisely what to do
with his or her partner, and the consequence was
the whole 16 either stood solemnly still or else
made aimless though spirited dashes to and fro, or
discussed the situation with one another. Miss
Brand occasionally stepped across to argue with
Her Excellent mamma, who stood passive beside
Mayor Ives—who, in his turn, appeared to be
rooted to the spot. Gov. Hampden certainly
darted to and fro like a hungry sparrow after flies,
and Mr. “ Seppy ” Stephen, dancing with Miss
Dorothy Brand, threw great verve into several
figures of eight which, no doubt, he had brought
with him from ’Ome—at any rate, I had never seen
anything like them in Australia before. But
nothing availed to save the Y.R. set from
being a wildly-comic incident. I understand
that a certain charming old lady who
is always chivvying people about was re-
sponsible for the fatal substitution of obsolete
quadrilles for lancers—and if so, I really think
she should have stood by with a wand and exer-
cised some of her well-known supervision. A
little gentle reminder such as “ Now, your Ex.
will kindly do a flip-flap, ” might have saved the
situation. And the odd thing was that the official,
to suit whose limited terpsichorean knowledge the
“ quads ” were set up, was far and away the worst-
fogged dancer in the set ! A lovely look-out for
Her Ex., who is doomed to be his official partner
nine times out of ten.
I was not impressed by the gubernatorial gown-
ing. Lady H. wore a black brocade with pink
roses on the corsage, and a few diamonds, dark
tan-colored gloves, and a committee bouquet,
which made a muddle of her toilette; Miss
Brand’s com. flowers (white and pink roses) were
ever so much more tasteful. The two V.R.
damsels were familiar in pink and cream frocks
respectively—but the Y.R. folks are not invariably
dressy; indeed, they sometimes look as if they
had periodical attacks of sackcloth and ashes.
They say that during a recent Y.R. visit to a
country show, the locals turned out a la Madame
Du Yan in silks and satins, so as not to be quite
wiped out by the Govt. House crowd. And, lo !
when the V.R. girls arrived they were clad in
washing print.
The F.A.L. committee-women were resplendent
in gay attire. The hon. treasurer (Mrs. Caro)
wore a new shade of coral satin brocade and dia-
monds, and one of the secretaries (Miss Richards)
was artistic in ivory pompadour silk, pink satin,
pink roses, and sea-green chiffon. Everybody was
in silk and (black) satin, which is going to be
fashionable this season after having been outlawed
ever since the notorious Mrs. Manning was hanged
in London in a black satin gown ever so long ago.
The fashion has taken a long time to revive in.
By the way, at a ball the other night a Sydney
society matron turned up with her raven hair
dyed yellow, and a Frenchy frock with plenty of
skirt to it, anyhow. She recalled the lady in
“The Importance of Being Earnest,” who got a
French dressmaker with the most marked results.
She also suggested that when the lady of a certain
age dips her cranium into peroxide of hydrogen
she may, after all, be only anxious to look the part
of a sere-and-yellow-leaf. ' And another golden-
haired matron in velvet was ecstatically pro-
nounced to be a “ picture ” by an impulsive girl
who really hit the mark. Pictures are mostly
painted.
To return to the F.A.L. ball —floor, music, and
supper were above reproach, and the decorations
were goodly barring those observable on the men-
folk, for, from His Ex. forward, the absence of
boutonnieres was almost disheartening. A button-
hole takes ten years off a man’s age, as they say in
“ Charlie’s Aunt,” and yet half the men at the
ball went without ’em. How different to the
stage ! “ Trilby,” for example, where all the gay
young men are uniformly beflowered with white
blooms, not to assert blamelessness but purely for
startling effect. I must confess, though, that I
consider the “Trilby” white boutonniere gets a
little overdone, especially as some of them are so
obviously paper.
The adorable Rev. Charles Clark opened to a
good house the other Tuesday at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, and was flatteringly received by a
well-dressed crowd who all listened attentively,
barring one young lady who had received a criss-
crossed letter from one of the Australian cricketers
at ’Ome, and who had apparently attended the
lecture to let the people see the famous signature.
Mr. Clark has accumulated a good deal more
waistcoat than when I last saw him, and his watch-
chain, therefore, hits you in the eye with greater
regularity than before ; but he is an imposing
figure on the platform, suggesting what Gov.
Brassey might have been if he had only grown
longitudinally instead of t’other way. The Rev.
Charles has picturesque grey hair, and a voice
like a lion when he likes, and even his fearful and
wonderful devotion to his Flag and his Queen
and the palpable way he barracks for Hands
across the Sea, and all the rest of it, cannot spoil
“ Old St. Paul’s ” —a soul-stirring account of the
good old days when the man with the heaviest fist
had all the say. By the way, Mr. Clark doesn’t
like the fashions. He devoutly wished the church
still had something to say in regulating female
attire, and expressed a belief that we would then
see the last of “ cart-wheel hats and balloon-
sleeves.” But how unjust! As for our big hats
I am sure many a Cardinal’s hat beats ours in
circumference and crude color, and, as for our
sleeves— didn’t we, in the first place, boldly take
the pattern for ’em off my lord the BNhop himself?
The intellectual sorrow of Sydney, “the
Women’s Literary Club,” held its usual meeting
in the society’s rooms on Monday. Papers on the
subject of the evening, “ The probable social
effects of ‘Looking Backward,”’ were read by
Miss H. and Miss P. A philosopher like Bellamy
has such different effects on different people that I
would really like to know what decision the club
came to about it. But really no woman should
ever look backwards. If she does she generally
finds the man she has just passed looking back-
wards too. And if he isn’t she is greatly dis-
gusted. And women have scriptural authority for
not looking backwards. Take Mrs. Lot, for
instance.
From W. H. Paling I have received the “ Par-
thia Schottische,” by Thos. E. Bulch. The
“ Parthia ” is a lively, grig-like musical trifle, none
the worse for being evidently inspired by “ Her
Golden Hair was Hanging down her Back.” A
“Souvenir d’Australie,” by E. Terlice, is a
“ piece” pure and simple, presentingno difficulties
to the most modern executant, and is only danger-
ous to life on account of a long glissando which is
liable to skin the digits of the unpractised ventur-
ing on it unadvisedly. And from the same firm I
am glad to receive another copy of Emile Ludlow’s
clever “Crescent” waltz, which has reached a
well-deserved fifth edition. Two vice-regally-
dedicated waltzes are “The Brand” (equivocal
name for an Australian composition !), by W. J.
Ellis, a dashing waltz with plenty of swing and
hammer about it; and “We Two ” waltz, by M.
L. Chambers, a carefully-written dance number
lacking, however, the spontaneity which ought to
wing the heels of dance music.
The Kennel Club show, held at Sydney Exhibi-
tion Building during last week, was one of the
most successful dog-gatherings ever staged in
Sydney—which is saying a great deal, for dog-
shows are popular things. Women like them be-
cause they can be sure of meeting most of their
male acquaintances there —for the dog is much
more than the friend of man—he is his idol, and
the apple of his eye thrown in. Perhaps man’s
devotion to dogs is the real reason why so many
marriageable girls try to look like Skye-terriers or
curl their hair in the poodle-vogue ? And, if you
notice, it’s generally the curly little women with
poodle profiles who go off quickest in the matri-
monial market. I know of one girl who won a
very respectable husband because of a poodleish
trick she had of balancing a biscuit on her nose—
and everybody will admit that the hash-house lady
who has a bulldog jaw stands a far better chance
of collecting her tithes from her male “guests”
than the landlady who looks like a moulting
canary. But these are digressions.
I can’t say I admire Gov. Hampden’s favorite
Dachshunds —long-bodied, long-legged monstro-
sities much like a Chinese idea of a bow-wow. I
preferred Airedales—one little black puppy of that
breed looked a very game baby. The fox-terriers
were numerous and of the same colors as usual.
Dog-shows are different to flower-shows in that
respect —they’re always doing something to flowers
to produce a novel color; but they never alter the
hue of dogs. If anybody produced a fox-terrier
with a pink skin and an elephant’s-breath patch
what a figure he’d touch ! But perhaps the colors
I mention are already in the market, for paint-box
names of colors are no use in speaking of dogs.
“ D’ye think he’s quite blue enough ? ” 1 heard a
connoisseur say before a large brindled animal as
grey as a badger.
The growth and spread of skinflintism in Aus-
tralia should be rapturously hailed by Anglo-
maniacs down-under as a sure sign that we are
becoming English very rapidly. When a rich
Australian first reaches England he spends his
money freely, but he soon draws in his horns on
finding that it is fashionable to be mean, and that
the very bluest-blooded plutocrats are the hardest
of nails. One Government House sets a splendid
example of old-world thrift—the latest instance
being when His Ex. decided to buy a pair of
carriage horses, and sent for a leading vet. to see
if he knew of anything suitable. The vet. of
course had the very thing, and H.E. expressed his
delight on all points until it came to the price—
three figures—and then his vice regal jaw dropped.
“Ai couldn’t think of payin’ more than £5O at
the outside,” he said in a horrified voice, adding
indignantly, “ Ai always understood hawses were
cheap in Austrailyah.” There’s not much profit
in selling to the Great now-a-days, who are like
country people come to town in their abject fear
of being “ taken down.”
At the recent marriage of Gov. Gormanston’s
cousin, Major Deasy, to Miss Marley, ex-governess
at Govt. House, Hobart Society looked on with
much the same expression, I expect, that a cat
wears when'll mouse escapes. The habit rapidly
gaining ground in vice-regal circles, of finding a
mate indoors, is considered almost unconstitu-
tional by marriageable Society, and is almost as
great a smash of unwritten law as though a matri-
monial agency should begin to make love to its
clients, obviously intended for somebody else.
Since this phase of inter-tribal marrying began to
boom at various Guvmentouses, match-making
mammas cast an anxious glance at a certain large
vice-regal party in which the women always swamp
the men, and decide that, if the epidemic sets in
there, somebody will have to commit polyandry to
make the suite go round.
The French ball, to be held at Paddington Town
Hall next Monday evening (15th), will be in the
presence of the Gov. and Lady Hampden, Mons.
J. Rigoreau, acting-consul for France, and all the
notabilities. Mrs. B. R. Wise and Mrs. Thring
are secretaries, and tickets are 7s. 6d. I under-
stand that supper will be served h la frangaise this
time. On Wednesday next the S.A.O.S. give a
grand concert in Sydney Town Hall with Signor
Hazon at the prow. A musical treat may be con-
fidently anticipated as far as the orchestra goes.
Miss Milly Wynn is to assist the society for the
first time, as a vocalist. On Monday, June 22,
the Suburban Bicycle Club and Darlinghurst
Harriers give their annual ball at Leigh House.
The Centennial Tennis Club dance is down for the
25th inst. (Miss Mowle and Miss Summerville
hon secs.), and on 30th June the Tempe ball takes
place in Sydney Town Hall, tickets for which are
selling like fat sheep.
Despite an excellent portrait of myself at the
head of this letter, there seems to be some doubt
abroad as to whether lam a man or a woman ! I
hear from Marble Bar (Westralia) that A. and B.
have a bet—A. wagers that I am a man ; B. that
I a woman. lam inclined to believe that B. wins.
But people really seem so uncertain upon the
question, on which I have once or twice overheard
heated arguments, that I sometimes get a little
shaky on the subject myself. Anyhow, to settle
the Westralian wager, I may say that I have every
reason to believe that lam a woman. I always
look under the bed at night for burglars, and can-
not hammer a nail—and these, mind you, are only
two of many triumphant proofs which I might
advance in favor of my own theory if space per-
mitted.
The presentation to Governor Hampden at
Grafton of a myall stick cut 30 years ago when the
Duke of Edinburgh raged on the Australian con-
tinent and never went a railway journey without
timorously inquiring if there were any Mayors on
that line —recalls the yarn lately revived in a
London journal, of Edinburgh’s second official
visit, when he made friends with the prettiest girl
in New South Wales, and consequently gave
Sydney the lion’s share of his time. When he
tore himself away at last, His Royal Meanness
presented the lady with a diamond bracelet in
token of the many pleasant hours passed in
her society. A copy of the letter which accom-
panied the gift was published in the daily
papers, making Sydney society rage as one
woman. But its mourning was turned into joy
when, by-and-bye, it leaked out that the stones
had been valued by a jeweller and had proved to
be paste! This, however, did not prevent the
canny (or cozened) Prince from putting down the
worth of the bracelet as .£2OO among his official
ex.’s when he got back to his bleeding country,
which “publicly refused to allow this demand,
which was privately settled out of the secret-
service fund. ” In the face of this characteristic
anecdote, it really looks as if Gov. Hampden’s
myall stick isn’t very marketable, or else the
greedy “ Duke ” would never have left it behind
him.
On dit that w'hen a perambulating and popular
vice-regality, who is not altogether deaf, dumb
and blind to the charms of womankind, was being
received with triumphal arches of gum-tree and
waving banners at, say, Mindaribba, a feature of
the badly-spelt decorations was a flag made out
of a petticoat, with “Welcome, Yere de Vere ! ”
blazoned upon it. The inference was —but I really
forget what the inference was. However, petti-
coat-flags are a bright idea, anyway—an economi-
cal method of utilising what threatens to become
an obsolete garment.
It was at the same, or a similar shivoo, that the
townsfolk were hugely entertained at the patroni-
sing air adopted by the mayor, who outbowed
His Ex. by a bushel of nods, though he ought to
have been hiding under the seat blushing at the
way “ Britannia ” was spelt all through his
borough (I think it must be called a borough,
because the mayor’s official robes are generally
trimmed w'ith rabbit-skin). His Worship and
daughter drove by in the mayoral carriage, each
bowing from right to left, amid the smothered
mirth of the onlookers. Bowing from the head of
a procession is the privilege of Governors, and
means just nothing at all. Its real utility is that
the uninitiated may easdy pick out the star in the
circus.
It seems that, despite his ardor and hard work
Gov. Lamington has not succeeded in catching the
coy goddess of popularity. In a country where a
working man sings out to the ’bus-driver ‘keep
the change,’ the grip of the bawbees that prompts
haggling over details of shillings and pence doesn’t
catch on with the multitude.

“ Coolgardie localities take their names from
Melb. suburbs. A Victorian girl, who lately went
there as a bride, writes that her charming wood
and-canvas villa in Hawthorn boasts three rooms
no yard, and a back-door, infested by stray
camels. In ‘ Toorak,’ she says, there are beautiful
four and six-roomed ‘ Mansions ’ for sale, which
of course, are too expensive. Meat is still Is. per
lb., cabbages fid. each, but money is not over-
scarce, and altogether it’s great fun.
“The plaintiff in a recent English upper-
crust slander-case spent her early married life
in a Bananaland town where her own sex did not
love her a bit. The Northern aristocracy, it
seems, incurred her resentment by giving out that
before ‘ he picked her up ’ she colored photographs
professionally down Sydney way. In return, the
sprightly Uitlander presented every woman in the
place with a nickname, calling one ‘ Nose,’ another
‘ Bunions,’ and so on. If the men hadn’t quoted
her ‘ impertinences ’ her victims would not have
cared a scrap, so they said. As it was !
“ Talking of Queensland ; some blackfellows up
there swam ever so far, the other day, to bring help
to a shipwrecked picnic party. They are to be re
warded by gorgeous brass-plates to hang round
their necks, and four of these baubles have been
ordered, though the heroes number only three.
Another darkie, a mate of theirs, would certainly
use his tomahawk if he were left out in the cold
just because he had nothing to do with the rescue.
So the donors have decided that, even at the cost
of a few shillings extra, it is better to buy safety
for their black benefactors.
“ The Austral Salon was quite cheery the other
evening, when Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis gave some of
her ideas on Shakespeare, interspersed with per-
sonal theatrical experiences. Such a bright, crisp,
interesting lecturette; a Salonite did well to
announce that it was the best woman’s paper ever
read there. Otherwise the official congratulations
were not too happily expressed. Both speakers
made so much fuss about the lecturess’s age, pull-
ing up here and there to ask her forgiveness for
saying she’d been here so long, &c. Not that a
blithe, bonnie, charming woman like their victim
has any cause to be sensitive on that score. It is
a good old-fashioned rule that ladies’ ages are not
to be proclaimed from the house-tops. Still, when
you have transgressed the law, mounting the
steeple to apologise only makes things worse.
“ R. S. Smythe wouldn’t give Gov. Brassey ill
a week as a professional lecturer. The little man's
oration on the Navy at Melb. Town Hall was th
dullest, stupidest, hum-haw-est, yawn-and-snooz-
lest piece of statistical flapdoodle mortal man ever
imposed on a tired audience. But, of course, no-
body paid anything to be present, and everybody
seemed inclined to tolerate H.E. because he is
supposed to be a naval expert, and it is the privi-
lege of experts to be bores on their own special
subjects. But, as for there being any interest in
the thing—why, the lecture was so leaden that its
delivery on board an ironclad would sink the
vessel in half-an-hour.
“ The truth is that the ponderous and humor-
less Gov. Brassey' is a most exasperating speaker
to listen to. He is slow —80 words a minute,
reporters say, is his average speed —and every
sentence is cut up with hum—hum — hum—haw-
haws, which are as little stools upon which his
mind sits down to think about what is to come
next. He chucks chunks of statistics at his audi-
ence, and if he has any argument t® round off,
with any neatness, never does it in his own words,
but drags in a quotation. At least one-fifth of
every speech he makes consists of quotations.

Mr. Donald Manson, the original repre-
sentative in Australia of the Waltham Watch
Company, of the U*S., and whose name was a
synonym for generosity and good fellowship, died
last week in Sydney of acute Bright's disease after
a very short illness. His remains were followed
to the grave on Sunday' by a large number of
sincere friends.

What the Wonderful New Invention Mat
Show Regarding Things We Already Know.
“It is an astounding discovery—one of the
marvels of the present age.”
It was in these words that an eminent scientist
lately referred to the new and wonderful invention
in photography which everybody is discussing.
This great invention, as most people are aware,
enables photographers to take pictures of objects
completely hidden from the sight. In a recent
experiment, for instance, a portrait of a man was
secured, although he was separated from the
photographer by a three - inch partition. In
another case, a picture was obtained of all the
bones, muscles, blood vessels, and internal organs
in a man’s body.
In speaking of this wonderful discovery, a well-
known physician recently said : “If thousands of
people who are walking the street to-day, and
attending to business, could only see a photograph
of their internal organs, they would be filled with
alarm and dread. They would see their liver
and kidneys clogged with impurities, and their
blood filled with deadly secretions which poison
the whole system. They would see these delicate
organs slowly but surely giving way under this
corroding, poisoning influence. Oh, what an
effect such a picture would have ! ”
The doctor is perfectly right, but at the same
time there are thousands of people who need no
photograph to tell them that there is something
wrong with their health. They know that they
do not feel as well as they ought to feel. They
complain of tired feelings, nervousness, headaches,
backaches, stomach troubles, constipation, and
dull, heavy sensations. They feel miserable, but
do not know why they suffer. It is only the
skilled physician who knows that these symptoms
are all caused by the liver and kidneys being out
of order. When these great organs of the body
fail to act properly, the health speedily breaks
down.
It is a knowledge of this important fact that
lead to one of the greatest discoveries of modern
times. For long years the brightest men in the
medical profession sought for a remedy which
would cure all these derangements of the liver and
kidneys, and at last they succeeded in finding it.
This great remedy, which has proven a blessing to
thousands, is Warner’s “Safe” Cure, a remedy
that to-day is known and honoured all over the
world. No other remedy has ever equalled it for
making weak, run-down people feel strong and
well. It is universally admitted to be the safest
and best remedy for curing every form of liver and
kidney disease. It puts an end to stomach
troubles, purifies and enriches the blood, and
restores lost vitality.
This great “Safe” Cure is not an experiment.
It has been in use for over twenty years, and has
been the means of saving millions of lives. No
other remedy compares with it for building up the
system and imparting new life to every part of the
body.*

England as soon as the doctors can persuade him
to go. It is quite time that the weary solicitor

off work before work knocks off Turner.

tralian interviewer begged him, as a favor, to
father 4 cols, of a “ policy for Australia ” which
he had invented, and which no paper would take.
Kipling weakly consented ; and was credited with
setting Australia right exactly 16min. after he
landed.

Young Dilke, son of the celebrated Eng-
lish baronet, came to Australia, it is alleged, to
buy a sheep-station —not to offer himself to the
shearers. In Adelaide he had another battle with
“the Count,” Oxenham’s partner, and had the
best of it, striking him for a <£3ooo double, Simu-
lator and Banjo. Dilke was the sensation of the
recent S.A. meeting. He is a bright, gentle-
mannered youth, who, sooner or later, will learn
that no punter who ever lived was a natch for the
Australian bookmaker —with the noble Australian
horseowner at the latter’s back.
G. Republican Dibbs, now at the head of
N.S.W. Savings Bank and out of politics, spends
much of his spare time at the lathe, turning out,
among other things, shirt-studs made of myall-
wood, which he distributes among his friends.
At Sydney licensed victuallers’ annual shivoo re-
cently he decorated Copeland’s bulgy shirt-front
with these articles to such an extent that after
lunch the expanded one looked like a moveable
hat-stand.

whereupon old Jack emp-
