tents of his pocket. * ‘ Very
unwise that, Sir John,”
said temperance - apostle
John Davies, who was
one of the party, “that
man will surely .go back
two miles to the public-
house and spend youJ
money in drink.” “I sup-

Davies,” said old Jack, “ if
he doesn’t he will be a
blanky fool. I know I
blanky well would ”

National Gallery three
mementoes of Parkes—the

by Ashton ; an ivory coupl-

Foster— not W.G. the
father of the Josephsons, &c.
Chief Justice Darley professes to be unable
to understand how Mr. Moodie became a member
of the Dean Defence Committee at a time when he
believed Dean guilty. When Mr. Moodie joined
the Committee, he said he did so, not for the sake
of proving Dean’s innocence or otherwise, but to
insist on the right of every man having a fair
trial. It was not Dean who was at stake, but the
liberty of the subject, and the necessity for having
it understood that no man on trial, and no jury,
should be domineered over by an arrogant Judge.

David Syme gets more absurdly “ loyal ”
as he grows older. From this year’s birthday-
leader in the Age. Behold :
Queen Victoria is a sovereign over a continent, over 100
peninsulas, 500 promontories, 1000 lakes, 2000 rivers, and
10,000 islands. She waves her hand and 900,000 warriors
inarch to battle in “ thin red lines,” as stubborn as those
in the historic past. She bends her head in acquiescence,
and 1000 ships of war and 100,000 sailors do her bidding
on the ocean. She walks upon the earth, and 400,000,000
human beings feel the pressure of her foot.
And, after all, this majestic personage is only an
old lady who gets <£385,000 per annum for pre-
tending. She is the fly on the wheel, and D.
Syme insists throughout two columns that she is
hauling the coach. Arise, Sir David !
Spalding and Airey, colonels, who retire
from N.S.W. military life at the end of this month,
served as a span of the bridge that connects Aus-
tralian soldiery with British. The British Secre-
tary of State at last declared firmly against longer
supporting soldiers in Australia out of the British
revenue, whereupon N.S.W. Parliament voted
.£20,000 to start a regular army of its own. Among
the first appointed were Spalding (then in Aus-
tralia on half-pay) and Airey—who visited the
Soudan in England’s “darkest hour,” and who talks
proudly from time to time of what might have oc-
curred if something which didn’t happen had only
taken place.
The murmurs of a threatening storm begin
to float on the placid surface of dull sassiety, which
does not appreciate “ thrift ” in a Guvnah reported
to be possessed of seven millions. H.E. is said to
have explained his lack of lavishness by a remark
that ‘ * the Victorians would have to learn that a
Governor didn’t keep up the style they expected
on a paltry £lO,OOO a year.”
“Be just and fear not” was the motto
inscribed on boomster Bent’s S. Melb. election
posters. The electors were just, and the conster-
nation fell upon Bent. But Bent is not yet
squelched. When told that he had forfeited his
deposit-money after the S. Melb. polling, he
replied, “Well, it is a poor recompense to the
State for the loss of my services.”
Notwithstanding the number and quality
of the late Sergeant-Major Shegog’s medals, Tas.
Commandant Parker refused to accord the Bala-
clava hero a military funeral. Recently a grocer -
Volunteer officer was buried with all the pomp and
glory of war: the whole city turned out. Few
followed Shegog. He retired as he had lived in
Tasmania—quietly and almost unknown.
A London letter-writer apprises a Sydney
friend that his Honor Judge Windeyer has reached
his old form in the pursuit of spiritualistic pheno-
mena. He says the on-leave Judge is personally
cultivated by several notable materialisers, and is
stronger in the ‘ * faith ” now than at any previous
part of his career. A special stance should be
arranged for the Judge to interview the Mt.
Rennie ghosts, with Stead to write it up.
When the late Maori king, Tawhaio, was
induced to go to England with a view to placing
his race’s land-grievance before Queen Victoria,
Her Majesty declined to see him. “No matter,”
said Tawhaio to a Maorilander whom he met in a
London restaurant and who won his heart by
addressing him in his own lingo ; “ plenty beauti-
ful wahines that I meet in the street ask me to
come and see them.”
In transcribing the cable re the Czar’s
coronation, no less than two Sydney dailies spell
“anointed” with a double “ n ’’ —“annointed.”
Which recalls an old story. When Sydney
Evening News, owned by the Bennetts, was at
fierce war with the equally-romanceful Stak, the
former paper, referring to its loathsome contem-
porary, used to constantly print the heading
“ Ananias Again ! ’’—spelling Ananias, however,
with a double “n ” : “ Annanias.” “ Why,” asked
someone, “does the News spell Ananias with a
double ‘n’?” “Pure egotism,” said someone
else, “ there is a double ‘ n ’ in ‘ Bennett.’ ”
Mr. Charles Moore, ex-Curator of Sydney
Botanic Gardens, owes the two great “ wrenches ”
of his life to Public Service Boards. Nearly 60
years ago he was happily settled in Dublin as
Botanist to the Ordnance Survey, but the Govt,
there started a Board of Commissioners to reor-
ganise departments, and by the advice of that
Board Mr. Moore’s office was abolished. He soon
afterwards came to Australia, and now again, in
his 76th year, another Board turns him adrift.
“It will be a great wrench to me to leave my
happy home,” he said the other day when the
notice of retirement reached him. Fifty years ago
the same words fell from his lips.
H. : “Melb. Collins-street Independent
Church has just held a Weep Meeting over the
permanent departure for England of Mrs. Be van,
esteemed wife of the pastor, with her family.
Anyone would think the Styx had intervened,
instead of the pastor finding Melb. not good
enough for the education of his offspring. And
let me say, mildly but firmly, despite all dis-
claimers, that Pastor Bevan is going too. He’s
sick of coffee-palaces, and has a strong nostalgia
for Highbury Quadrant. He came at <£3o a week,
but what he gets now, goodness knows—he hardly
does himself.”
The Victorian sculptor, Bertram Macken-
nal’s, Show-Sunday, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent from London, was crowded by the sort of
people who “ take you up ” and dare not let you
down afterwards. If you are a duffer, so much
the better and easier for you ; but if a genius the
takers-up are contented to point at you as a
growth of their own nurturing. The Australian
showed three busts—two marble and one plaster.
The former, Mrs. Herring and Mrs. Hart, society
dames ; and the latter, Miss Marie Tempest, a
leading actress. Mackennal is recognised as a
“ very coming ” man indeed.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Geographical politics. X.S.W. is badly
off for navigable rivers, and worse off for navigable
roads.
X.S.W. Leg. Council has let the Home-
stead Selection and Settlement Leases Bill slide
down the toboggan of a no-quorum count-out.
It will be a piece of luck or pluck if the Bill is
heard of again this session. Bat Man sleeps on
business principles.
The English Bar—acting in its own inte-
rests, of course—has prohibited sons of Judges
from practising as barristers before their fathers.
Australian Bars are all too frightened of the
fathers to follow suit; but, with this precedent
added to the conclusive existing case. The Bulle-
tin hopes to see Parliamentarv action taken at
once.
W. M‘Millan, M.L.A.. is, an observant
politician opines, making a bee-line for X.S.W.
Agent-Generalship. A troublesome thorn in the
ades of all par ties, all will conspire to shelve him.
The Speakership was not a Protectionist gift to
Joe Abbott. The Freetraders were eqnallv glad
to lock him up. And such, when the vacancv falls
in, may be the story of M'Millan.
The Argus, which never tires of condemn-
ing the justice of the tax on Land \ alues, prints
on its jubilee a facsimile of its first issue (June 2,
1846 which contains a host of Govt, advts. of sales
of land in the nearer suburbs of Melbourne at
upset prices of usually less than JE6 per acre. The
expression, “Waum chain of ponds,' 5 in this fac-
simile calls to mind the almost forgotten fact that
" Moonee Ponds is an abbreviation of the
'• Moonee Moonee Chain of Ponds 55 of Fawkners
day.
The Q. revenue, according to Govt, re-
turns, is advancing by leaps and bounds, and
that province (so Ministers assert) is the most
solvent of the group. Such being the case, one
can scarcely understand the cheese-paring economy
by which, in some districts, the whole of the male
adult aboriginal population has been deprived of
its customary dole of one shoddy blanket per
annum. Xo wonder Q. niggers are vowing, to
the amusement of Xorthern humorists, “Me go
longa Labor-party. 55
A Sydney daily newspaper, feeling itself
in the place where it must speak the truth, has
twice tried to sound in its leading columns the
correct note on the Parkes Parliamentarv
Grant question. In the first instance, it with-
drew the article at the eleventh hour and substi-
tuted a rigmarole on foreign politics, and in the
second instance it hung up the “revise” and
handed over to the printers a stock article on pump-
kins or something. So the public are still with-
out that “ correot ” note.
X. bee-keepers have apparently onlv
just awakened to the fact that the wholesale
destruction of the country's timber-producing
trees is using up its natural sources of honey.
Lands ' Carruthers has argued the matter fur-
ther —Kill trees, abolish honev; abolish honey
destroy honey-feeding birds; extinguish honey-
feeding birds i also insectivorous ), and vou breed
the various kinds of bugs and things that eat up
Hie vines, orchards, crops, &c. So that a new
Ringbarking, <fcc.. Act may be looked for.
A pretty constitutional question is being
mooted re the effect of the Federal Council’s legis-
lation on provinces joining the concern after the
passing of the legislation. The Act re judgments
pa?>cd in b 6, and South Australia joined in
88, so it would appear that, as S.A. could not
have “referred’ the Act to the Council, and, as
it has not apparently expressly adopted it, this,
■which is one of the few useful Federal Acts
passed, is not in force in South Australia.
Another instance of the parlous state of the law
regarding interprovincial judgments.
Queensland is fortunate in owning a half-
dozen Radical journalists, full of energy and
enthusiasm, who have dotted the province with
half-a-dozen little journals ever on the alert to
expose the plots of Boodlewraith. And these
lively little local papers—self-supported—are be-
ginning to cut into the prestige of the subsidised
Brisbane V\ orker, which is a well-meaning organ,
but far too dull. By the same token, workers up
North and Centre are beginning to resent Brisbane
domination, however gentle, and are inclined to
treat with the metropolis rather as allies than as
subjects.
Melbourne Metropolitan Fitzgibbon Board
°f Works has one graceful way of evading its own
minimum-wage clause. Said clause provides that
a certain wage shall be paid on all contracts for
“manufacturing” goods for the Board, but it
doesn t come in when the contract is simply for
“ supplying " goods. When the Board called for
tenders for the manufacture of drain-pipes the
contractor had to at least make a pretence of pay-
ing the minimum-wage. Now it simply calls for
the “ supply ” of the same articles, and the wage-
question doesn't come in at all. A beautifully -
delicate distinction.
Dear Bulletin. —Copeland quotes “Alton
Locke, and does not know the authors name!
Copeland aspires to be the leader of X.S.W. Pro-
tectionist party ! Give us in preference, say,
Chumpkins, -Jones, Bill Smith, of Gone Bung
Gully, or anyone who will give us semi-illiteracy
without pretentiousness. Dibbs did read some-
times—Lyne does by proxy—Copeland (like Reid)
never reads. A reader, as everyone knows, is not
necessarily a thinker, but the man who never reads
rarely thinks. Let the Freetraders have a mono-
poly of the non-reading and non-thinking leader-
ship.—" Fours, Splittem.
Sydney D.T. :
There is intrinsically no difference between adding a
pound sterling to a man's grocery or drapers' bill and de-
ducting the same amount from the money that he has to
pay it with.
The Freetrader forgets that his policy shuts “ a
man” (the factor in this argument) out of work,
and causes him to have no money at all and no
chance of getting any honestly. No money, no
grocery-bill. And you can't deduct a pound from
nothing. There is a big intrinsic and practical
difference between the addition and the reduction
here. The D.T. merely states a false equation.
Dear Bulletin, —The conservation of the
people's park is a brave cry now that the extension
of Sydney city railway is again hotly discussed.
Curiously, the private capitalist and land-and-
house-owner, willing to surrender space for a
route, stand side by side with the green-in-his-eye
patriot at this particular juncture. And still
another band of conjurors started last week to
whisper into the long ear of the same green-in-the-
eye patriot that Government House and grounds
should be resumed for a depot, and a residence in
lieu be provided for the Governor in one or other
of the suburbs. Just so. In Carington's day
three of these suburban properties were placed
under offer to Government, and a couple of per-
sons, believing trade would take place, went bank-
rupt in their haste to become middlemen. Fully
a dozen owners of “ spacious grounds ” are now so
desirous of accommodating the Governor, that the
cry for resuming his present residence promises to
become a boisterous element in the railway-exten-
sion discussion. —M.

There appears to be a flaw in the Vic.
Constitution Amendment Act which would
occasion a serious disfranchisement- if a-n unscrupu-
lous Conservative Government were in power.
There is a provision which keeps the old electoral-
rolls in force after they have nominaflv become
obsolete up to the meeting of the Revision
Courts (a period of about two months), but no
provision keeping the electors’ rights in force. A
sudden dissolution with elections in Januarv
would, if this view be correct, disfranchise all the
manhood-suffrage electors in the countrv. This
should be seen to in the forthcoming amendment
of the Purification of Rolls Aet.

The Tic. Crown Law Department has no
rival in the art of how not to do it. The Rapi-
port-Baxter fiasco is the latest tribute to the
Department s genius, which was already proved
by the Mercantile Bank case. Daviess nolle
prosequi was simply an annihilation of C.L.D.
work by the Crown'sown Attorney-General. The
next attempt to bring Matthew to trial ended
in a quashing of the Crown’s grand-jury decision.
Anon, when the sundry Mercantile Bankers had
been brought to trial by a private prosecution, the
Crown stepped in. took control of the case, and
ignominiously lost it in a maze of objections and
overridings. Away further back there was the
Imperial Bank case, a famous instance of small
men being nabbed whilst bigger men escaped
prosecution. Likewise—but, no matter. The
Victorian C.L.D. doesn't want The Bulletin, or
or any o h-r journal, to enlarge upon its merits.
Apparently the Victorian revenue will be
£300,000 less than was anticipated, which
tnat the old deficit won’t be reduced after all, and
that by tough eeonomy ends may just aboutstoeet,
or again they may not. The drought ruined the
grain -carrying trade, and this alone made nearly
5£200.000 less railway revenue. The abolition of
the primage duties meant a loss of .£30,000. Some
riotous alterations which the Assembly insisted on
making in the tariff caused a further loss of
revenue; and the rich men have been, on the
whole, very healthy, so the probate duties have
been lamentably small. The trouble might have
been got over, after Premier Reid’s fashion, by
charging a lot of the ordinary public works expen-
diture to loan funds, but this hasn’t been done,
so the usual Vic. deficit may again be heard of.
Consequently, the Turner Government is believed
to be pondering further taxation, and the Council
is sharpening its teeth on the grindstone in antici-
pation.
The Vic. Government has decided, after
innumerable boring experiments, that in 16,600
square miles of the mallee country —about one-
fourth of the entire province—there is no artesian
water whatever. The soil is porous, and most of
the rivers are absorbed before they get to any-
where in particular. • Some of the lakes are salt
and shallow ; the rest are fresh and shallow ; and
all of them dry up on the shortest notice. It is
reckoned that all this vast district will require to
be irrigated, at a cost of about .£1,000.000 or
more, betare it will be inhabitable to any extent
worth mentioning; and there is so very little to
irrigate with that the project bristles with diffi-
culties. There is room for some heroic engineer-
ing about the mallee lands. Possibly when thingß
are so much improved that artesian wells can be
sunk a distance of six or eight miles engineers
may come on deposits of fresh water that will put
an entirely new complexion on things. If they
don’t, the next best hope is that the art of rain-
making mav yet be discovered. And. failing that,
the time will yet arrive when the Murray and its
tributaries will have to be used entirely for irri-
gating the dry lands of Victoria, X.S.W., and
and navigation will be shut down upon
altogether, and Australia's one big river will never
reach the sea at all.

Hannam and Co., whose water-heaters are
among the simplest, most economical and service-
able requirements of a household, have removed
to 148 Pitt-street, near King-street, Sydney—a
fine central position which should suit the firm as
well as the whole circle of the population. All
that needs learning about the working of a water-
heater can be learnt either by a minute’s inspec-
tion at 148 Pitt-street, or by looking over the
illustrated catalogue (post free). The water-heater
is prized by everyone who uses one; it raises the
temperature of winter water to 155° in two
minutes. Sir G. R. Dibbs says: “Hannam’s
water-heater is a very useful addition to the bath-
room ; no family should be without one.” Mr.
Badgery, of Messrs. Pitt, Son and Badgery, says :
“I do not know of anything which adds so much
to the comfort of a family as Hannam’s water-
heater does.”*
Visits to the dentist are, even at this
enlightened day, associated in most minds with
harrowing experiences. The victim of the irre-
pressible molar has still fearsome thoughts of being
yanked about a room at the end of a pair of tongs,
at the other extremity of which is a perspiring
but muscular toothsmith. That was how things
used to be, but a great change has come over the
business of late years. U nder the new and perfect
system adopted by the London Dental Institute
(of 98 King-street. Sydney) the operation is utterly
painless. In fact, it is rather a pleasure than
otherwise. Also in the bad old days fabulous
sums had to be paid to the bunglers who laid your
mouth up for alterations and repairs for a period
ranging from six months to the term of your
natural life. The London Dental Institute
charges rates the moderation of which can only
be gauged by paying them. The operators
employed are all armed with diplomas which are a
guarantee in themselves that all work performed
will be of a perfect and permanent nature. All
artificial teeth supplied are admitted to be of a
description to cause natural ones to turn green
with enyy, and are guaranteed for five years.
Also, a medical officer is attached to the staff.
Th ese and innumerable other advantages should
be borne in mind by the ache-racked client whose
tooth has gone to wreck, and whose molar has
departed.*

Adelaide epitaphs “ Newhaven ” Symonds,
assistant to Deputy Surveyor-General Light in
laying-off that city in ’37. With him dies a wealth
of early reminiscence, for he, of the few old hands,
had the memory that could revive in graphic de-
scriptions the early scenes and struggles of S.A.’s
pioneers. Symonds’s little canker was his failure
to realise a fortune out of Newhaven, the proposed
township he laid out at North Arm, Port Adelaide
way. He was a generation or two in advance of
his time.

The ordinary business man’s most critical
difficulty just now is how to get his money in. In
the recovery of debts, especially in circumstances
of difficulty or delicacy, Holdsworth & Co., 101

The case of Austin Kirby, the Maoriland
detective who absconded from his bail on a black-
mailing charge, isn’t calculated to make the
Sydney force feel “good.” Kirby apparently
stowed himself away in a big packing-case on
board the Talune, and though the vessel was
searched on arrival and officers remained on board
while the cargo was being discharged, he peace-
fully stepped off into space. It was only ascer-
tained that he had been in the packing-case
because the name of a suspected accomplice was
Erin ted on it, and inside it the lamentable accused
ad left a bag of biscuits, two loaves, two bottles
of water, and a bank-draft with his name on it.
Then, instead of lighting out for Thargomindah,
Kirby resolved to double back to Maoriland per
the next steamer, and the Sydney detectives
ably discovered his scheme because a wharf-
laborer tramped up to the office and told
them about it ; and when they got down
to the vessel they identified their prisoner
partly by a malformation of the hand, and partly
by his extraordinary height. When somebody
doesn’t walk right into the detective office with
the address of the wanted person, then the force
simply plods around in its regulation boots with a
noticeable air of not wanting to attract attention,
and asks questions in a loud whisper at three-
penny and other pubs. His obtrusive unobtru-
siveness is sufficient in itself to giye the ’tec away
most times.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Prince of Wales Avon the Derby with Per-
simmon, “ hot pot” St. Frusquin, owned by Leopold de
Rothschild, finishing second, and it goes without saying
that “the first person to congratulate H.R.H. on his
popular victory ” was the loyal subject aforesaid. Also,
it is possible that the owner, trainer, and jockey of St.
Frusquin winked their other eyes at one another, whilst
Albert Edward was acknowledging the greasj' plaudits of
the multitude. Persimmon may have won the Derby on
its solid merits, for the race was the fastest on record.
On the other hand, Rothschild is very rich and exceed-
ingly devoted to the Royal Family. And the natural
feelings of the horse must be taken into consideration.
When it came to a question of blasting Albert Edward’s
hopes, or making the Heir Apparent truly happy, St.
Frusquin hesitated and was lost —The Bulletin suspects.
* » * *
Persimmon, by St. Simon —Perdita 11, is so
neatly named that “Wales” surely can’t have christened
it. Per, the first syllable of the dam’s name, also Latin
for “ by” ; Simmon, for Simon, in cockney dialect. Per-
simmon, a handsome fruit. Good enough to take first
prize in a nomenclature competition.
* * * *
Jockey Watts has his reward. After making
up his mind to retire on a handsome fortune, he was per-
suaded to put in another season of self-denial for the sake
of Persimmon’s Derby chance, and he squeezed the ’orse
home by a neck. Whereupon Watts, presenting his
saddle and bridle to the Coming K., should have sworn
off riding for evermore. An opportunity for a gaudy
exit.
* * * »
Penciller Sam Allen got a second-hand sort of
reply from Tummy Wales’s private-secretary, in reply to
his Derby-win congratulations. A.J.C. Secretary Clib-
born’s message was signed “Albert Edward,” and he
posted it up on Randwick grandstand.
» » * *
Another phase of the “ superlatively auda-
cious” attempt of the Y.R.O. to annex the flat at
Flemington was entered upon the other day by a confer-
ence between the club and the neighboring municipali-
ties. The public have used this ground for recreation for
35 years, but lately the club have fenced it off and have
been exercising the rights of owners. This conference
was arranged as a result of various protests, but effected
nothing beyond showing the public that the “ flat” is no
longer theirs. It adjourned for a fortnight to enable the
representatives to ask their councils “ whether they
would be willing to forego access to the flat if the com-
mittee would favorably consider a proposal to throw open
the heights and road along the bank of the river ? ”
The puzzled “public” wants to know what
power any councils have to “forego” its rights in this
matter, and also when the Govt, is going to step in and
stop Mr. Frank Madden and the Y.R.O.’s high-handed-
ness? Councillor Schutt, of Williamstown, a stout de-
fender of public rights in this matter, asked Madden what
were the claims of the V.R.0., and was told that “the
committee would resist any application for throwing open
the flat to the public.” The dangerous part of the busi-
ness is that the Flemington Council, the most directly
interested, is apparently willing to barter the rights of
the public in this matter for a mess of pottage for itself,
in the shape of a concession by the V.R.C. of a portion of
their ground for a Flemington public recreation reserve.
• * * *
This year’s big Cup list is 27 less than the ’9l
total. Five horses placed in previous Cups appear—Hova,
Jeweller, Devon, Nada, and Ronda.
• * » *
That big Burrabari Melb. Cup bet, published
recently, appears to have been a tug-enticing expedient.
The horse isn’t entered. Bookies owe a lot to the daily
press.
* * * *
The folly of racing Auraria at the Y.R.C.
Autumn Meeting is seen in the omission of the mare’s
name from the Melbourne Cup entries. Auraria went
wrong mysteriously, and a breakdown which can’t be
promptly located is a hopeless case, as a rule.
* * * *
It is fully 5 to 1 against Redleap going to the
post for the V.R.C. Grand National, but not 5 to 1 against
the giant winning either the Hurdle or Steeple—with a
start. The gilded Millers would hardly be such shabby
sportsmen as to run Redleap unless he were fit and
seasoned for the journey.
* * * *
O’Trigger is a horse worth thinking about for
future events. By Trenton, out of Barley, he is a beauty
to view, and, if Power can keep him going at hard work —
which is doubtful —he may shake one of the Cups.
* ♦ * *
At least four big Cup horses were entered out of
curiosity to see what weight they would get.
* » * *
Response, favorite in a toney field of 19, ran ex-
ecrably in Randwick Stakes. It was a good betting event,
but some of the best-backed were unable to get on the
scene in time. Ruenalf ran nineteenth.
* * * *
A hurdle-jock, whose mount went a cropper on
Saturday at Randwick, got the “ half of twelve” for his
peculiar riding before the accident.
» * * *
The hair-line of fineness to which handicapping
may be brought received illustration at Epsom (Vic.),
when Hera and Last Glen dead-heated twice for Trial
Stakes, Hera winning by a neck only in the third run.
There was a 211 b. difference in their weights, and half a
brick more on either would have sent the money one way
or the other.
* • * *
The jockeyed field in A.J.C. Randwick Stakes
on Saturday showed more galloping energy than usual,
the genuine anxiety exhibited by so many to win being
engendered by the all-pow'erful sweep inducement. It
was a pound to a pin against any of the rear-guard head-
ing the front seven at the distance, and good biz. to back
any of the leaders at that point. The fast-finishing True
Blue got home by enough to swear to in front of Hindoo,
the colt thus hooking an unexpected double, having
brought off the Rapid Stakes earlier. True Blue was
penalised 101 b. in the lj-mile, and gave away weight all
round to a first-class crowd.
* * * *
It is little wonder that people are diffident
about betting much over obstacle events on Sydney race-
courses. The fields are generally small, and the number
of triers smaller. Most of the events consequently smack
of the much-faked selling swindles. Stewards should
scarify on last Saturday’s lines. The man who brought
his ’chaser up at a Melb. Cup pace, when he knew he
couldn't win, deserved his little year. He is a most in-
artistic loser.
» * * *
True Blue, a slashing galloper, threatens to give
his young sire, Hotchkiss, a great advt. The colt’s double-
event at Randwick was an eye-opener.
* * * *
V.R.C. men have been remarking on the
decrease in Cup entries. What encouragement do they
give long-distance racing? In their programme to-day
(Sat., 13th) the big handicap is only a mile. Any crock
ought to go that distance, so good ’uns and stayers are
perforce not entered. A mile spin as the principal attrac-
tion brings the V.R.C. down to proprietary-course level.
* * * *
Whenever the ring gets struck persistently by
the public it points bitterly to the bad handicapping. An
ideal man who could frame an unpickable list every time
would be at once installed as Ikey Mo’s joss.
* * * *
Presence of mind ! Sir Henry was winning
Epsom (Vic.) hurdles easily, the other day, when Chummie
suddenly thundered alongside and beat him by a head. The
titled horse lost through his rider groping round for his
Avhip when the crisis came. It was in his mouth 1
» * * *
Reinsmen with mud-splashed reputations should
not wag an idle tongue. On a train-ride lately a shady
jock, let off a few choice observations re a certain Melb.
rider. An oldish-looking listener objected strongly.
[‘What do you know about him?” queried the puller.
“ Well, I’m his father, and if he had your character he’d
cut his throat.” The conversation then lapsed for want
of a talking quorum.
• • • •
A jockey, accosted at Rand wick by a lady with
a rather familiar face, was asked about the horse he was
to ride. “ Well, between you and me, mum, you had
better back something else.” “ Why—not good enough ?”
9 , be’ s good enough, but ” (significantly) “ I don’t
think he’s quite well.” “ Thank you very much. So glad
I know.” Then the lady tripped off and met her husband,
who owned the ’orse. The nature of their dialogue hasn’t
transpired, but that jockey has since learned the identity
of the lady.
A certain owner, hearing his prad was to be
seized for debt, persuaded the obstinate lawyer, after an
hour’s talk, to wait two days. “He had a monte on, and
the lawyer could have a big wager himself ; it was a cer-
tainty.” The good thing duly won, and the shark made a
cosy heap. Next day he got a cheque for the debt, but,
unsatisfied, wrote for £7 odd, legal costs, interviews, &c.
Then debtor sent in his little bill: “To advising you to
back ‘ Ringtail,’ £1 Is. ; to advising you again in re same,
£1 Is. ; to further advising you to have a bit more on just
before the start, £1 Is. ; to 10 per cent, on winnings
secured through me, £7 10s.; total, £lO 13s. Please send
cheque for balance due, and oblige.”
*»' * *
A sweep-yam : A mug, with little knowledge
of racing, wrote a trainer, asking to see the horse
he had drawn. Request agreed to, trainer ascertaining
meanwhile that the lucky one didn’t know a bean from a
broom. Desiring to make a good impression on his visitor,
and get a big bit to nix, he trotted out a peacocky prad,
all show' and shine, instead of the plain-looking real
Mackay. “What do you think of him?” “Is that
him?” “ Yes.” “Oh ! he’s all right, but the last time I
saw him he was a chestnut! ”
* * * *
The Christchurch Jockey Club first used the
tote in 1880, since which it has expended £185,000 and
paid the State £3OOO in Totalisator tax. In ’BO the club
could only afford to spend £5000; last year it spent
£16,000 and had £2045 surplus.
* * * *
That convicted Sydney bank-embezzler’s <£lo7o
plunge represented a three weeks’ flutter, and must have
quite set up the small “ books.” The wretched clerk, w'ho
didn’t back a single winner, says he got all his “good
things ” from the daily papers.
* * * *
£lOOO in stakes for the two days’ Coolgardie
meeting. Coolgardie Cup £3OO, Goldfields Cup £lBO, and
Hurdle-race £l2O are the chief prizes.
* * * •
<£119,525 was put through the tote in eight days
at Adelaide meeting. Onkaparinga holds the one day’s
record with £18,136.
* • * *
At Otaki (M.L.) races Mystical, beaten by a
nose, would have paid a div. of £l9O. Bona Fide, the
third horse, would have paid £122, and the fourth horse,
Springtime, nearly £6O. As things were, each investor
on the winner drew £2O 19s. The tote does not ask its
patrons to wait, “as the race has been a bad one;”
neither does it barrack them to pick a double in part-pay-
ment. But then it doesn’t drink champagne and clothe a
harem in sealskin and diamonds. M.L. Ikey Mo when
“ betting tote-odds” limits the dividend to £lO or £l5.
* * * *
A bookie who complained to his grocer, the
other day, about the rise in the price of butter was
snappily told to go and raise the price of his odds. And
the grocer himself didn’t see the point of the remark
until an hour afterwards.
* * » *
Turner can’t be deriving much selfish satisfac-
tion from Australian Eleven achievements up to date,
though it would really appear that, to put it mildly, he
would be at least as well in the team as M’Kibbin.
* * » »
The Gentlemen of England were seemingly not
very anxious to take the shine out of the invaders. The
match, Australia v. The Gentlemen, fixed for 18th inst.,
has been abandoned for the insufficient reason that six
counties will be starting important matches on the same
day. Swell amateur influence would have got over the
difficulty, if so disposed. An eleven picked from the
Midland counties is to fill the gap.
* * * *
Granted fine weather, the Australians should
have a magnificent “gate” for their London d£but at
Lord’s to-day (11th). The M.C.C. will probably put a
first-class representative team in the field.
» * * *
Young Townsend, the Gloucestershire bowler,
doesn’t seem to have troubled the Australians much.
Darling and Donnan knocked up 80 runs before the first
wicket was taken by—E. M. Grace, the 50-year-old “ lob-
ster.” As for Gloucestershire batting, well, eight men
made 30 runs among them, including sundries.
* * * *
Melb. Football Charity Saturday’s play (gate,
£200) again demonstrated the superiority of Essendon.
After defeating Collingwood by 3 goals (all kicked by
Kearney) to 2, in a 40min. game, the Essendons came out
again and put Port Melb. through by 2 goals to 1. They
were hardly troubled in either game, but their match to-
day (13th) with S. Melb. should about see them at their
best. * * * *
Sydney didn’t empty itself on to the football
grounds on Saturday when the Wentworth-Wallaroo
tussle was the only one worth gate-coin. It was an
obstinate, pig-headed ground fight, and the Went.’s even-
tually rejoiced by a goal to nil. The dangerous Wallaroo
backs got no open work in the first spell, Went.’s making
it all scrum. But with the wind behind afterwards they
reversed their tactics. The passing and tackling were
superb, but Woller, Went.’s back, will lose more than he’ll
catch collaring high. “ Collar low ”is the acknowledged
Rugby war-cry.
* * * *
Randwick’s victory over Pirates by 35 points to
love not only shows how good the winners are, but how
much the Pirates have deteriorated since last season. They
have got pluck, but want practice —together. Paddington
went under, as usual—this time to University by 22 to 0,
all scored in the first spell. Paddington, with wind
advantage, ran over its opponents afterwards, but had
no line-crossing luck.
* * * *
S. W. Stanley, the “ coming champion ” billiard-
player some 15 years ago, has gone the way of most of
them. That is to say, he’s dead before his time, whilst
grey-haired Roberts, who figured as actual champion long
before the plucky little S.W. had grown out of knicker-
bockers, still goes on improving his game. Stanley was
the younger brother of “Dave” Richards (another bil-
liardist), both of them, for some reason, having chosen to
drop their family name —Digges.
• • • •
Although vaguely asserted to be impracticable
by an interested section, the urgent necessity for a turf
by-law regulating eleventh-hour erasures for races over a
fixed value cropsup at every horse-fixture of importance. It
is painfully obvious afterwards that certain animals have
been kept in merely to fatten Ikey Mo and the stable at
public expense. Some owners, who have no show or have
two entrants, put a laying-commission in the market, or
get one, secure the return, and then scratch after the fat
has been skimmed. But the much-milked crowd will yet
be struck all round once too often. And then they’ll cry
for the tote awake and asleep.
* 4c * *
The “electric bicycle” is said to have arrived.
A Frenchman is alleged to have patented a bike which
carries a dynamo under the seat. In the new machine
the foot pedal movement regulates the speed, but does
not generate it as at present. Once fairly set going, the
electric bicycle only requires steering. Its inventor
claims that it can be made to go faster than anything
ever known in the world of wheelery, and that a man
might ride it at top speed practically for days without
fatigue. Which, if it be true, looks like a revolution in
the bicycle trade.
There will one day be a great "exposure in both
Melb. and Sydney on the line of the Melb. Jaypee scandal,
as to the running of bagnios, Chinese lotteries and totes.
Mesfcon’s mission up in Cape York Penin-
sula should be productive of good. If the idea of
establishing food-stations for the aborigines had
been adopted years ago much loss of life on both
sides would have been prevented and Bananaland
would be a lot in pocket into the bargain. It
is calculated that it would have less to
provision the remnant of blacks in the North and
give them a decent reservation than has been ex-
pended in maintaining that gang of dusky des-
peradoes, the Native Police.
Two pars., found side-by-side, in Sydney
D.T.:—
Owing to the cheaper price of flour under freetrade in
New South Wales, a Victorian-border baker has started to
make his dough at Albury and bake it in Victoria, thus
escaping the duty.
During the past few days, full}' 180 tons of vegetables
have been imported by rail from Victoria into New South
Wales.
Thus it will be seen that, when one small baker
sends dough from N.S.W. to Victoria, it proves
that Freetrade has made bread cheap in N.S.W.
And when a lot of people send 180 tons of vege-
tables from Vic. to N.S.W., it doesn’t show that
Protection has made vegetables cheaper in Vic-
toria —at least, it doesn’t show it to any extent
worth mentioning.
Sydney Daily Telegraph, in last Tues-
day’s leading article, barracked vigorously for the
Lands for Closer Settlement Bill, and pointed out
enthusiastically that there was no danger what-
ever about the powers conferred on the authorities
to “ resume ” the property of its brother Bill at a
price which might be six times that authorised by
Parliament. Says the D.T. :
Under the Lands for Public Purposes Acquisition Act
the Executive have power to resume private properties to
the extent of hundreds of thousands of pounds, behind
the backs and without the concurrence of either House of
Parliament. With such powers as these in the hands of a
Minister or a Ministry, the possibility of corruption might
be urged ; but no such accusation has ever been seriously
made, and we fully believe that during the whole fifteen
or sixteen years of administration of the Lands for Public
Purposes Acquisition Act not a single improper use of
that Act has been made.
Why, certainly ! There was never any bad smell
attached to the Darling Island resumptions or the
purchase of the A.S.N. property, was there ? And
no one ever dreamt of supposing that a Govern-
ment, under whose regime thousands of public
money were spent in making roads through a
Minister’s property, could possibly be suspected
of any tendency to wink the other eye. And the
idea that all the railway resumption business has
been one continuous stench from first to last is
only a vision, of course. Considering the approval
which the Closer Settlement Bill has evoked from
so many quarters, what a number of people there
must be in N.S.W. who hope to have their land

The miners of “ Golden Gippsland,” living
mainly on tick, porcupine and bandicoot, now
that the luxurious mushroom season is almost
over, have found that sun-dried water-cress is the
only decent substitute for tobacco.

An Italian sneak thief sent to gaol in Mel-
bourne the other day seems to have followed the
example of his brigand fellow-countrymen who ask
a blessing at the local church every time they set
out on a raid. Among Maccaroni’s papers was a
sheet giving details of his mode of living. One
sentence stands out: “I have in the Savings
Bank £ll, to which—with the help_of God and the
Blessed Virgin Mary—l will this vveek be able to
add, say, £3, bringing the total up to £14.”
The gallows on which the Boer patriots
were hanged by the British at Slaughter Nek in
1815 is still extant, and is preserved by the Boer
who owns it as a holy relic. When Jameson was
captured, the Boer packed up the aged instrument
of death and hurried with it to Pretoria that it
might be ready for “ Dr. Jim’s ” execution, but
the event didn’t come off. Then he offered it for
the hanging of Col. Rhodes and the other Johan-
nesburg insurrectionists, but that didn’t come off
either. Consequently a piece of poetic retribution
missed fire.

A person with a fine strain of the humorous
aboriginal about him, was lately “sent up” for
three months at Auckland (M.L.). He was a
hulking individual who lived upon one of the
“ fallen women ” of the city ; that is to say she
kept him in board, lodging, clothes, tobacco and
drink, and was regularly and freely walloped in
return. Probably if she hadn’t been walloped she
wouldn’t have stuck to her owner with so per-
sistent an affection. In between times he made
the miserable girl stand in a corner while he aimed
open pen-knives at her outstretched arms, and at
other times he fired hat-pins at her cheek from
across the room. Judging by the scars upon her,
and by the fact that he hadn’t stabbed her in the
eye, the court decided that he was a tolerably good
shot with a hat-pin. Also, he used to make her
hold a large piece of tobacco in her mouth for an
hour at a time, and if she became ill before the
hour was up she was spanked or trodden on.
Finally one night, he struck a perfectly new idea,
and after severely whaling his Flossie he got
her head between his knees and made her
swallow twelve packets of salts in one
act. This drastic treatment doubled the
wretched creature up, and for the first time she
revolted; she had taken the hat-pins and the to-
bacco and the canings and all the other things as
part of the day’s work, but the twelve packets of
salts were too much. The result, as already men-
tioned, was three months. In the same province
and the same week an old man of 70 got 12 months
for vagrancyj but that is another story. The
offence of being old and insolvent is one thing,
and that of filling a girl with 12 packets of salts
is something quite different.

Sight Examined Free of Charge.
Dear Bulletin, —Minister Carruthers is
tangled re the West Bogan Scrub Lands. The
lessees say the terms are too severe. Monetary
institutions don’t see their way clear to the out-
lay. Instead of 2/6, some of the pine-scrub will
cost 10/- per acre to cut and bum, and 6d. to 1/-
per acre a year to keep down. Then there are the
mallee and box seedlings to cope with, and they
are just awful. From time of scrubbing and burn-
ing off it will be at least 3 years before the land is
at all fit to stock. In 4 years more, some honest
selector comes along, taking advantage of the im-
proved state of things, and reaps the benefit
thereof. Lessees say it’s too sudden, especially as
all improvements fall to the Crown. The Land
Board sat for several days at Nyngan recently
wading into the matter. A prodigious amount of
evidence from surveyors, inspectors, and others
was taken. Summed up : all agreed it is really
and truly “Scrub Land.” But scrub is not the
only trouble of the unfortunate West Boganite.
Needle-grass threatens to become a plague—even
wallabies won’t eat it. It tears the wool off the
sheep and smothers them with seed. The fact is
these scrub-lands should have been classed in the
Western Division, if tjje framers of the Land Act,
or the Parliament which made it law, had under-
stood the matter they weredealing with.— Nyngan

A true tale of Northern Queensland.
Scene, decayed township, consisting chiefly of two
public-houses. One publican’s wife is a chronic
drunk, and when drunk has a long-standing
grievance against wife of the other publican. Gets
drunk, as usual, and goes over to reflect at the
top of her voice on the character of the opposition
lady. The opposition lady comes out, tears off a
good deal of her friend’s hair, and gives the wreck
into custody. Result: three months hard in the
gaol in adjacent town. A few months later drunk
repeated. Another great free fight between the
two public-house ladies. First lady again reduced
to a wreck and given in custody, and gets three
months. Next day a party starts per coach for
the adjacent town. Mother in custody, and hand-
cuffed as she shows signs of violence, en route to
begin her spell in gaol. Daughter and in-
tended son-in-law on their way to be married
at town on the road, and casually seeing
the old lady part of the way to gaol at
the same time. Husband going to assist at
the wedding and also casually seeing the old
lady part of the way to gaol. Marriage comes off
successfully. Festive wedding-supper ; opposition
publican and his wife—the lady who had twice
knocked out the bride’s mother and given her in
custody- both present; also the J.P. who had sent
the old lady up. Reunion, flow of soul, and
general drunk. Newly-married pair live happily
for two months, and then the son-in-law belts the
old man in the course of a family row, and leaves
the pub. to set up a house of his own. Quantity
of property also disappears. The old battered
publican tries to lay information against his son-
in-law, but the latter is now the only J.P. in town
(the other one having fallen into a creek) and re-
fuses to issue a warrant against himself. Story
closes here, as the old lady is only half through
with a third spell of three months in gaol. N.B. —
A certain monotony attaches to this true narra-
tive, as it consists almost entirely of the same old
lady getting three months in gaol.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the widtli
round the joints AA, round the instep Bit, round heel CO,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities— a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send.to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price,
POST FREE to any part of Australia or N.Z.

Vic. Government intends next session to
submit an Act which will give the State absolute
control over all exported produce. At present it
can only refuse to put the Government brand on
bad butter, cheese, fruit, &c., with the result that
these goods are sent along “on their own.” Then
it sometimes happens that they turn up mysteri-
ously at the other end with a bogus stamp on them,
and the Govt, brand, which was meant to be a
guarantee of excellence and to ensure that Vic.
produce would bring the best price in the market,
loses a lot of its value. The new idea means that
inferior produce will be prohibited from leaving
the country at all, which will make Vic. exports
sommand the best prices going, and compel the
Vic. producer to brace up and stop turning out a
bad article. Theoretically the idea is a first-class
outrage on the liberty of the subject, who has an
unquestionable right to send away dirty butter
ind decayed fruit if he wants to, and thus bring
Vic. goods into disrepute. Practically, however,
It has a lot to recommend it.
Cynicism—Enthusiasm dipped in experi-
ence.

Pretty soon newspaper reporters and pen-
women will need to band together for protection
against the pale snufflebuster who is tempted to
creep stealthily into their godless profession. A
shorthand-class for either sex has been formed in
Melbourne, the fee including membership of the
Y.M.C.A. or Y.W.C.A., “with all privileges”
appertaining thereto. If stenography is to fall
into the flabby paws of early pietists, journalism
will do worse than go to the devil. It will become
converted until the character of the average para-
graph is above suspicion, and the bold cross-
heading of to-day is replaced by an announcement
that “ Deeming has found Rest in the Lord,” or
“ Bill Sikes has Sinned Again.”

THE BIG DRUDK AT DRUMMONDS
Darkness had fallen over the long, sandy
itretches of beach on Drummond’s Island. One
oy one fires were lit and gleamed among the
aouses and canoes that lined the western shore.
Women emerged from the dwellings with unlit
torches, and placed them beside the boats of their
husbands, about to go in quest of flying-fish—
murmuring the while, in their harsh tongue, a
chant to drive away the devils of the night. To
the people of most of the Kingsmill and Gilbert
islands the night is full of terrors, of un-
known demons and lurking dangers. And,
above all the other natives in the group, the
sun-tanned, wild-eyed man of average Taputeuea
(Drummond’s Island) is, in the broad light of day,
talkative, vociferous and quarrelsome; at night,
unless in the glare of a fire, sullen, morose and
timid.
Such were they when Wilkes, the American
commodore, had his bloody encounter with them
at Utiroa, and such are they to this present day.
Many white men have lived among them. Some
died violent deaths. Others, protected by power-
ful chiefs, and married to women of the island,
came through safely and lived and traded there in
peace—except when a ship came, and they bought
square gin. Then there was usually trouble.
* * * *
Three women were hastening away from
the canoes to the houses ; a fourth, throwing her
bundle of torches upon a canoe, hastened after
them, her grass airiri swishing to and fro about
her knees. She was fat and clumsy, and cursed
the three for leaving her.
“ Whither goest, pig-face, in such haste ? ” she
snarled to one of the others, a young woman,
placing her grimy, toil-worn hand on the girl’s
smooth shoulder.
The others laughed. “Pig-face, “Rat-eye,”
and “Shark-mouth” are, in the Line Islands,
analogous to Dr. Johnson’s alleged definition of
certain uncouth words as terms of endearment
among sailors.
“To the white man’s. Come there with us and
see. Timi (Jim) and three others are ashore from
the ship ; they have opened a box of te rom (gin),
and have musio; and Timi hath sent word to
Tibarian for all the men of Utiroa to come and
dance in view of his doorway.”
“ Hah,” grunted fat Riungo, “ and here are we
four fools carrying torches to the canoes. I tell
thee there will be no fish to-night. What man
will go a-fishing when that devil of a white-man
bringeth a red box of te rom to his house and
sayeth ‘ dance ’ ? ”
“ Hold thy peace, old fish-eyed devil! If the
men drink and lie down like hogs we can at least
steal some biscuit; for Timi’s wife and the wives
of the other men will be drunk, too. ’Tis our
ohance—hasten.”
* * * *
The four white traders were “having a
time.” They had sold their copra to the German
barque at a good price, been paid in cash, and for
once in their lives came ashore sober—because the
German skipper, bearing in mind his one previous
visit to the island, was careful to give them noth-
ing stronger to drink than lager-beer. One com-
bined purchase from him he delayed producing
till the last moment, when it was hoisted up from
the hold and lowered into the boat—four cases of
gin.
The captain took very good-naturedly the
sarcastic allusions made by the traders to the
tendency of the Teutonic nation in general to
accumulate fat in the head, and presented each on
leaving with a box of cigars. But he heaved a
sigh of relief as he saw the boat, paddled by a
crowd of fierce-eyed Taputeueans, shove off and
head for the shore.
* * * *
“ Tell you what, boys, said Jim, who was
playing an accordeon. “That Dutch skipper
ain’t a bad sort, after all. Here we are, enjoyin’
ourselves in A 1 style, all nice an’ friendly an’ not
a angry word passed yet. Now, if we’d opened
that square-face aboard the barque, where’d we be
now ? Help yourselves. ”
“Drunk, Jim, drunk as pigs, that’s where we’d
be,” said one of his guests, essaying with unsteady
hand to light a cigar. “Yes, he’s all right, an’
we might have been a bit more civil. You was the
man that called him a fat-headed Dutchman,
k i, , , . ,
Well, what if I did . said the big man, stop-
middle of an air from L Afncame.
Why, tain t gentlemanly, said a dark long-
faced man. Haow would yew like to be told
that your grandfather was hanged for smuggling
id -tiull .
There was a sudden scuffling of feet, and the
four men dosed for a minute or two. Then the
faithful and watchful wives and the native on-
r l ls “ e( J m . an ~ separated them.
Mutual apologies followed. Iwo empty bottles
were taken oft the table and a fresh one opened.
Then the big man sang a song—something about
daisies and a rustic bridge—and they all grew very
sentimental.
* * * *
The dark-faced man had just finished an
air on a blackened old banjo, when a sudden idea
occurred to the big man, suggested by a broad
gleam of moonlight falling upon the crowd of
natives that clustered around outside the house,
With somewhat unsteady steps he gained the door
and spoke to them.
“ How’s the tide ? ” _
“ Coming in,” they said; “ what about it ?
u Mian tno boat,” replied the big man, and
mind your own business. ” _
A submissively-chorused E rai rai Timi ( It
is good, Jim ”) answered him, and then the trader
turned to his white colleagues.
* * T . * „ * .. ~
Boys, look here. . It s a nne moonlight
night, an’ the tide’s coming. Let’s all go aboard
again and sweeten up the Dutch skipper. We’ll
all make him apologies an’ spin him cuffers.
Maybe he’ll come ashore with us an’ we’ll fill him
up. Are ye on ? ”
But the women objected. Te kdpeni (the cap-
tain) would be asleep. Besides, they were drunken
men (here an indignant denial from one of the
accused), and it was wrong for them to go away
and leave their wives with all this liquor in the
house, and so many natives from the other village
knocking about to try and steal it.
“That’s all right, my beauties,” said Jim —
“ we’ll lock up the house and take you all along
with us—and a case of liquor, too—in case we
misses the ship,” he added with a laugh.
* * * *
The women grumbled, but obeyed. They
are pearls of great price, these savage Line Island
women—unquestioning obedience and unswerving
fidelity to the white man are to them a second
nature. So they gathered themselves and their
children together and followed the four traders
down to the boat. The big man took the steer-oar
and the lug sail was set. The* breezy trade wind
whistled across from the ocean through the narrow
strip of cocoanuts that lay between the weather
side of the island with its roaring, thundering
surf, and the placid waters of the lee.
“ Ho, children of my heart,” said the big trader,
slapping on his tattooed back the native nearest to
him, “come and steer, the while I give thee all
some rom.”
A bottle was taken out of the box and each
native given a stiff drink. Then the four white
men lit fresh cigars and, lying in the boat,
watched her cutting her way over the moonlit sea.
She was of German build, long, narrow-gutted,
heavy, and utterly unable to beat but under her
big lug-sa-il and with the wind well aft she slipped
quickly through the water,
* * * *
Two miles off they could see the Saturn
lying head to wind and right ahead, the rays of
the moon tipping the tracery of her spars with
lines of silver. In another ten minutes the boat had
d over the wes tern reef and met the faint in-
COHling ocean swe ll.
“ Timi ” called out a native who satin the bows;
« let us paddle as weU as an d get to the ship
quickly.”
1 << yyjY 9 »
“Look.” He pointed astern. They lazily
turned their heads and looked towards the shore,
and saw that the long, dark line of cocoanuts and
white shining beach were covered by a squall of
i n ky blackness, bearing down upon them from the
eas tward
“Tarnation; there’s rain in that as well as
wind ! Paddles out, lads, and let’s try and get
aboard before it catches us. Give ’em all another
good rousin’ nip, an’ we’ll have one ourselves to
keep us from catchin’ cold.”
Now, when a Kingsmill native has had two
half-tumblers of strong gin he is either ready to
be merry or fight. In this case it was the former,
and the wild crew, plunging their paddles into the
seeth of the flying waters, broke into a wild chant.

TH E SANITAS CO.,
THREE colt lane.
Bethnal green, London.
And the white men, just drunk enough to be ami-
able with the whole world, urged them on with
loud hurrahs.
* * * *
Presently the first puff of the squall struck
them and the boat leaped to it and shot along
like an arrow.
“ Timi,” called out the man in the bows, who
knew what he was saying, “ we cannot get to the
sin]) before the rain and the wind are upon us.
Let us take down the sail, and bring her head to
the wind till its strength be gone.”
‘‘ Why so, fool, ” asked the white man, “ art thou
afraid of the rain or thy skin?”
“ ’Tis not the rain I fear, Turn. There is much
wind coming and but little rain ; and we have no
oars in the boat, only these paddles. It is better,
I say, for us to lie-to, head to wind. See, already
the sea breaks, and with paddles alone we cannot
regain the ship if we fail to get alongside when
the wind is strong as this.”
“Give that talker another drink of rom,” said
the big trader, “and let him hold his tongue, an’
let’s have another ourselves.”
The native pushed the glass aside, and glared
angrily-at his master.
Another bottle was opened and finished.
* * * •
Two men, with rifles in their hands, were
keeping anchor-watch on the barque. The one
for’ard saw the squall coming and called the mate,
and, at the same time, reported a boat right ahead.
Always needlessly apprehensive of the people of
Drummond’s Island, the mate roused out the cap-
tain, and in a few minutes every man was armed
and tremblingly expecting an attempted cutting-
off party.
In a few minutes the first puff that had struck
the boat whistled through the rigging of the
barque, and as she strained at her cable the white
horses bumped and thrashed up against her bows,
and sped away astern.
Presently the captain, glasses in hand, turned to
the mate and said :
“ Send the hands below, Stunner. It’s only the
white men coming aboard. Drunk, I suppose.
Here they are, close to. Stand-by there, someone,
with a line ; and damn them, I say, for coming
aboard at this time.”
A sailor stood by ready to heave a line. Then
they heard the notes of an accordeon playing
“ Farewell, my ole Kentucky home.”
* * * *
After the first few drops of rain the black-
ness of the squall lifted and the wind tore fiercely
from the eastward. In the boat, which was now
racing down before the driving sea upon the
barque, merriment and harmony still prevailed.
Haif a cable length from the ship those on board
saw no attempt was being made to take in her
sail. In the bright moonlight she sped down, now
rising on the top of a long swell, now sinking in
the trough. In another minute or so she was
abeam, and the big man, still playing his accor-
deon and bawling out something that was never
understood by those in the barque, passed with his
white companions and native crew away to the
westward —n u, 'er again to be seen alive of men.
* * * *
“Poor devils,” said the German captain ;
“ I suppose they saw they couldn’t safely come
alongside without filling, and kept on for the fun
of the thing, meaning to pass under our stern and
bear up to the south’ard, where they would get
under the lee of the land again. I suppose, though,
they found she wouldn’t do it, and had to keep
dead before the wind. If they have water in the
boat, and any food, they may have a chance.”
* * * *
Three months afterwards a boat drifted
ashore on the weather side of the Sir Charles Hardy
Group, fifteen hundred miles to the westward.
There, huddled together in the bottom, was a
dreadful mass of decaying mortality.
In the locker of the stern-sheets lay an aceor-
deon.
LOUIS BEOKE.
W.Q. cattle-owners out Hughenden way
profess little alarm about the Coming Tick. Say
they had him (also her) two years ago, and the
nuisance gradually died out.
The Westralian telegram-muddle hasn’t yet
been unmuddled, but people have got tired of
talking about it. One of its chief causes is the
absolute incompetence of lots of W.A. telegraph-
officials. When an operator is sacked in the
eastern provinces—for drink, stupidity, neglect of
duty, anything—off he goes to Hip Van Winkle-
land by first boat, and duly sends next month’s
W.A. Govt. Gazette for his cronies to spell out
his name in.
A swagwoman on the way to South Aus-
tralia was arrested in Bendigo the other day as a
vagrant. She had tramped from Sydney. The
Bench discharged her with a caution. Woman
may now, without much loss of dignity, even in
the eyes of Fossildom, travel across Australia on a
bicycle if she pleases, but when she takes to trudg-
ing along on her own proper legs, she is looked
upon as a vagabond.
The Mayor of South Melbourne and
a brother councillor made a futile attempt
to remain in the To wn Hall whilst the election-
votes were counted, and the returning-officer
seems to have genuine cause of complaint against
the police for not obeying his instructions to sling
the municipal intruders off the premises. Subse-
quently the returning-officer would have an-
nounced the result from the usual balcony of a
first-floor room, but found himself shut out by
order of the Mayor. Which petty revenge was
quite in keeping with civic dignity.
The latest “assaulted female ” up to date
lays her accustomed accusation against a Vic.
engine-driver. A train was leaving Dandenong
just as dusk was becoming night, when a large,
shrieking she-person swarmed up on the locomo-
tive and started to claw the driver and fireman.
Then she danced a fandango on the foot-plate, and
wanted to drive the engine herself, and yelled
“Fire” and “Murder.” The two officials ulti-
mately got her down, and took turns at sitting on
her till they reached the next stopping-place,
where she was given into custody. Next morning,
when she had calmed down somewhat, she laid
the regulation charge of assault, but it was
immediately dismissed from want of evi-
dence. The department, however, in its mildly
wooden fashion, has suspended the driver for
contravention of bye - law 714£ prohibiting
him “taking any stranger on his engine.”
Which is a warning to the next driver, when a
frantic lady climbs on his locomotive while in
motion, to straightway heave her off and let her
drop on her ear upon the ballast. It is remarkable
how a department can decide, after a week’s calm
thought in an office, what a man ought to do at a
moment’s notice when a wild-cat person, who
seems to have dropped from the clouds, starts
biting him on a moving engine in the dark.
“How the movement is spreading,” writes
a Hannan’s (W.A.) correspondent to The Bulls
tin. “I saw to-day a woman working a con
denser, and a few yards further on— more remark
able than all—a female ‘ dry blowing.’ We have
clerks galore here. . . . At church last night I
counted 95 men and five women. Different
Melb. or Adelaide.” Ir0B1
Whitehead, aguard on the N.S. W. Southern
line, while engaged in shunting a truck from his
tram a few weeks ago, slipped, and the truck went
over one of his hands, all but severing two fingers
Rising, he called out to a porter to sprag the truck'
Had he not done so, a bad smash would have
occurred. Then he attended to other duties and
got his train in order before asking permission to
go to the hospital to have the injury attended to
He has since had the fingers amputated.
One of the chief duties of a Judge is not
to know anything that isn t formally communi-
cated to him in court. Therefore, in the ’Possum
Dean divorce-case, N.S.W. Judge Simpson
remarked interrogatively to petitioner: “Your
husband was not hanged, Mrs. Dean?” and
petitioner replied that he wasn’t. Evidently if
he had been hanged, and his wife was still in want
of a divorce, he must have been hanged verv
badly. J
The cheap and docile Kanaka broke out
again, the other day, at Bundaberg (Q.) when 20
Malay tans “lay for” and murderously chopped
two Gilbert Islanders. They had no quarrel with
these particular islanders, but sometime previously
another Gilbert nig., who was whooping loosely on
the highway, had scared a horse which damaged a
Malaytan woman in running away, and though he
solaced the bruised lady with £1 damages the
tribe felt called upon to go out with its axe. The
Kanaka murderer and the leper are now the two
most reliable institutions in the Great Scaly Land.
Dear Bulletin,— “ A well-known clergy-
man has started a labor church in Melbourne.’
—Argus. Has he? The only labor church
I ever saw (excepting among some Oriental
and African peoples) was in Lanark, Scotland.
There Rev. James Manson borrowed the Town
Hall on Sunday evenings and held service therein.
The title of admission consisted in not possessing
a bonnet or coat, and old Malison’s own clothes
were of the shabbiest, although he was a married
mail. On Wednesday he had a prayer-meeting
for miners at Rosebank, working dress insisted
on. Moreover, he received no stipend, built a
church and paid an assistant to preach in it
Apropos of pay, I found on an ash-heap in Urana
an account-book labelled—“ Church.of England
Stipend Fund. Names of Subscribers who have
Paid.” There were no names in it. The custom
in Urana is to present the parson with a buggy.
He always sells it on leaving so as to plead poverty
in his next billet. That buggy is an income to
the johnny-all-sorts and the coach-painter. Par-
sons don’t stop long in Urana.— Scotty the
Wrinkler.
A Society Romance .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Although Maud Armadale and Cyril Bak-
trax had been sweethearts since childhood, their
love was not pneumatically tired, and meddlesome
relatives were constantly breaking up the roadway
for them. Cyril was a poor clerk too lowly graded
in the bank to have opportunities for any brilliant
dishonesty. Maud Armadale had the rich dower
of rare beauty which her parents felt satisfied
could, with judicious management, be boomed in
the matrimonial market. As is the custom with
parents in the very best society, Mr. and Mrs.
Armadale put every objection in the way of the
union of the lovers ; but Maud was a Victorian
girl, and, although she did not belong to the
Austral Salon, had a will of her own. When she
was laying in a stock of husband, she determined
to do her own sampling.”
“If I am going to marry,” she told her father,
“ I am going to choose the man for myself.
In stolen interviews with her lover she repeated
her resolve and punctuated it with kisses. She
would wait for ever —even longer —for him until
he had saved enough to take her to see Pastor
Kinsman. Then he told her that, though it wrung
his heart-strings to leave her, he had an offer to go
to Westralia and that he could soon make a com-
petence.
And she swore to be true to him through all the
years to come, and that wild horses could never
drag her to the altar with any other man than
Cyril.
» * * *
The months flew by. The usual treachery was
played on Maud. Cyril’s letters were intercepted
and, eventually, ceased. Maud was hurried round
the various shambles of the marriage meat-market.
Many offers were made, but she remained firm to
her early love. Time and absence, however, did
their work, and the persistence of the young
squatter, Lambswool, prevailed. A year after
Cyril’s departure she consented, to the undisguised
delight of her parents, to become the squatter’s
wife.
On the day before that fixed for the marriage,
Maud sat looking dazedly at a telegram telling of
Cyril’s return, and his intention to call on her
that day. All the remorse of a traitress began to
throb in her breast. What could she plead when
Cyril learnt the ghastly truth ? All the old love
rushed back upon her as she heard his ring at the
bell, his well-remembered footstep in the hall.
“ Maud! ”
“ Cyril ! ”
Then there was a terrible silence. He had
evidently learned of her approaching marriage,
yet hesitated to begin his reproaches.
“ You have been fort i ~: in the West, Cyril ? ”
she asked tremulously.
“Beyond my wildest expectations,” he replied.
“I am wealthy—and I—l have come to —to tell
you ”
“Stop,” she shrieked wildly, “Oh, kill me,
Cyril, in mercy kill me ! lam to be married to-
morrow.”
A glad light leaped into his eyes as he seized
her in his arms.
“My own true girl; and I was married last
month.”
And, as he folded her in his embrace, the
shadow of the divorce-court loomed large upon
the re-united lovers.
